While the first two volumes advocated radical reform, the third adopted a <a href="/wiki/Social_conservatism" title="Social conservatism">socially conservative</a> stance, proclaiming that while men and women were equal, they were biologically designed for different roles in life.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198796.E2.80.93100Vandewalle200819Kawczynski201124Bruce_St._John2012161.E2.80.93165_118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198796.E2.80.93100Vandewalle200819Kawczynski201124Bruce_St._John2012161.E2.80.93165-118"><span>[</span>116<span>]</span></a></sup> During the years that followed, Gaddafists adopted quotes from <i>The Green Book</i>, such as "Representation is Fraud", as slogans.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012162_119-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012162-119"><span>[</span>117<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, in September 1975, Gaddafi implemented further measures to increase popular mobilization, introducing objectives to improve the relationship between the Councils and the ASU.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012165_120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012165-120"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>These radical reforms led to discontent, furthered by widespread opposition to the RCC's decision to spend oil money on foreign causes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200818Kawczynski201123_121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200818Kawczynski201123-121"><span>[</span>119<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1974, Libya saw its first civilian attack on Gaddafi's government when a Benghazi army building was bombed.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987114_122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987114-122"><span>[</span>120<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1975 two RCC members, Bashir Saghir al-Hawaadi and Omar Mehishi, launched a failed coup against Gaddafi, and in the aftermath only five RCC members remained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987118Vandewalle200818Kawczynski201123Bruce_St._John2012165_123-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987118Vandewalle200818Kawczynski201123Bruce_St._John2012165-123"><span>[</span>121<span>]</span></a></sup> This led to the RCC's official abolition in March 1977.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012165_120-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012165-120"><span>[</span>118<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In September 1975, Gaddafi purged the army, arresting around 200 senior officers, and in October he founded the clandestine Office for the Security of the Revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987118.E2.80.93119_124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987118.E2.80.93119-124"><span>[</span>122<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1976, student demonstrations broke out in Tripoli and Benghazi, and were attacked by police and Gaddafist students. The RCC responded with mass arrests, and introduced compulsory <a href="/wiki/National_service" title="National service">national service</a> for young people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987119.E2.80.93120Vandewalle200818Kawczynski201123_125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987119.E2.80.93120Vandewalle200818Kawczynski201123-125"><span>[</span>123<span>]</span></a></sup> Dissent also arose from conservative clerics and the Muslim Brotherhood, who were persecuted as anti-revolutionary.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987122.E2.80.93123Kawczynski201129.E2.80.9330_126-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987122.E2.80.93123Kawczynski201129.E2.80.9330-126"><span>[</span>124<span>]</span></a></sup> In January 1977, two dissenting students and a number of army officers were publicly hanged; <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> condemned it as the first time in Gaddafist Libya that dissenters had been executed for purely political crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987121.E2.80.93122_127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987121.E2.80.93122-127"><span>[</span>125<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Foreign_relations_2">Foreign relations</span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaddafi_1976.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Gaddafi_1976.jpg/170px-Gaddafi_1976.jpg" width="170" height="214" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Gaddafi_1976.jpg/255px-Gaddafi_1976.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/28/Gaddafi_1976.jpg/340px-Gaddafi_1976.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1072" data-file-height="1352" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaddafi_1976.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi in 1976 with a child on his lap.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following Anwar Sadat's ascension to the Egyptian presidency, Libya's relations with Egypt deteriorated. Sadat was perturbed by Gaddafi's unpredictability and insistence that Egypt required a cultural revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198688BlundyLycett198774.2C_93.E2.80.9394Kawczynski201166_128-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198688BlundyLycett198774.2C_93.E2.80.9394Kawczynski201166-128"><span>[</span>126<span>]</span></a></sup> In February 1973, Israeli forces shot down <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Arab_Airlines_Flight_114" title="Libyan Arab Airlines Flight 114">Libyan Arab Airlines Flight 114</a>, which had strayed from Egyptian airspace into Israeli-held territory during a sandstorm. Gaddafi was infuriated that Egypt had not done more to prevent the incident, and in retaliation planned to destroy the <i><a href="/wiki/RMS_Queen_Elizabeth_2" title="RMS Queen Elizabeth 2" class="mw-redirect">RMS Queen Elizabeth 2</a></i>, a British ship chartered by American Jews to sail to <a href="/wiki/Haifa" title="Haifa">Haifa</a> for Israel's 25th anniversary. Gaddafi ordered an Egyptian submarine to target the ship, but Sadat cancelled the order, fearing a military escalation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198687BlundyLycett198782.E2.80.9383Kawczynski201166.E2.80.9367_129-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198687BlundyLycett198782.E2.80.9383Kawczynski201166.E2.80.9367-129"><span>[</span>127<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi was later infuriated when Egypt and Syria planned the <a href="/wiki/Yom_Kippur_War" title="Yom Kippur War">Yom Kippur War</a> against Israel without consulting him, and was angered when Egypt conceded to peace talks rather than pushing for total victory.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198687Kawczynski201167Bruce_St._John2012182.E2.80.93183_130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198687Kawczynski201167Bruce_St._John2012182.E2.80.93183-130"><span>[</span>128<span>]</span></a></sup> Sadat and Gaddafi became openly hostile, the latter calling for Sadat's overthrow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201167_131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201167-131"><span>[</span>129<span>]</span></a></sup> When Sudanese President <a href="/wiki/Gaafar_Nimeiry" title="Gaafar Nimeiry">Gaafar Nimeiry</a> took Sadat's side, Gaddafi turned against him, and by 1975, Gaddafi was sponsoring the <a href="/wiki/Sudan_People%27s_Liberation_Army" title="Sudan People's Liberation Army">Sudan People's Liberation Army</a> to overthrow Nimeiry.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987185Kawczynski201179.E2.80.9380Bruce_St._John2012191_132-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987185Kawczynski201179.E2.80.9380Bruce_St._John2012191-132"><span>[</span>130<span>]</span></a></sup> Focusing his attention elsewhere in Africa, in late 1972 and early 1973, Libya invaded Chad to annex the uranium-rich <a href="/wiki/Aouzou_Strip" title="Aouzou Strip">Aouzou Strip</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987181Bruce_St._John2012187_133-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987181Bruce_St._John2012187-133"><span>[</span>131<span>]</span></a></sup> Offering financial incentives, he successfully convinced 8 African states to break off diplomatic relations with Israel in 1973.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198688Kawczynski201177Bruce_St._John2012184_134-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198688Kawczynski201177Bruce_St._John2012184-134"><span>[</span>132<span>]</span></a></sup> Intent on propagating Islam, in 1973 Gaddafi founded the Islamic Call Society, which had opened 132 centres across Africa within a decade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986103.E2.80.93104BlundyLycett198793.2C_122Bruce_St._John2012186_135-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986103.E2.80.93104BlundyLycett198793.2C_122Bruce_St._John2012186-135"><span>[</span>133<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1973 he converted Gabonese President <a href="/wiki/Omar_Bongo" title="Omar Bongo">Omar Bongo</a>, an action which he repeated three years later with <a href="/wiki/Jean-B%C3%A9del_Bokassa" title="Jean-Bédel Bokassa">Jean-Bédel Bokassa</a>, president of the <a href="/wiki/Central_African_Republic" title="Central African Republic">Central African Republic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201177.E2.80.9378_136-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201177.E2.80.9378-136"><span>[</span>134<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi sought to develop closer links in the <a href="/wiki/Maghreb" title="Maghreb" class="mw-redirect">Maghreb</a>; in January 1974 Libya and Tunisia announced a political union, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Islamic_Republic" title="Arab Islamic Republic">Arab Islamic Republic</a>. Although advocated by Gaddafi and Tunisian President <a href="/wiki/Habib_Bourguiba" title="Habib Bourguiba">Habib Bourguiba</a>, the move was deeply unpopular in Tunisia and soon abandoned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198776Kawczynski201171.E2.80.9372Bruce_St._John2012183_137-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198776Kawczynski201171.E2.80.9372Bruce_St._John2012183-137"><span>[</span>135<span>]</span></a></sup> Retaliating, Gaddafi sponsored anti-government militants in Tunisia into the 1980s.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201172Bruce_St._John2012183_138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201172Bruce_St._John2012183-138"><span>[</span>136<span>]</span></a></sup> Turning his attention to Algeria, in 1975 Libya signed the Hassi Messaoud defence agreement allegedly to counter "Moroccan expansionism", also funding the <a href="/wiki/Polisario_Front" title="Polisario Front">Polisario Front</a> of <a href="/wiki/Western_Sahara" title="Western Sahara">Western Sahara</a> in their independence struggle against Morocco.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201171Bruce_St._John2012183_139-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201171Bruce_St._John2012183-139"><span>[</span>137<span>]</span></a></sup> Seeking to diversify Libya's economy, Gaddafi's government began purchasing shares in major European corporations like <a href="/wiki/Fiat" title="Fiat" class="mw-redirect">Fiat</a> as well as buying real estate in Malta and Italy, which would become a valuable source of income during the 1980s oil slump.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986114BlundyLycett1987199.E2.80.93201_140-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986114BlundyLycett1987199.E2.80.93201-140"><span>[</span>138<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Great_Socialist_People.27s_Libyan_Arab_Jamahiriya">Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Foundation:_1977">Foundation: 1977</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg/220px-Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg" width="220" height="146" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg/330px-Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg/440px-Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg 2x" data-file-width="615" data-file-height="409" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Gaddafi_with_Yasser_arafat_1977.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi with <a href="/wiki/Yasser_Arafat" title="Yasser Arafat">Yasser Arafat</a> in 1977</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 2 March 1977 the General People's Congress adopted the "Declaration of the Establishment of the People's Authority" at Gaddafi's behest. Dissolving the Libyan Arab Republic, it was replaced by the Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya (<a href="/wiki/Arabic_language" title="Arabic language" class="mw-redirect">Arabic</a>: <span lang="ar" dir="rtl" xml:lang="ar">الجماهيرية العربية الليبية الشعبية الاشتراكية</span>‎, <i><span title="Arabic transliteration" class="Unicode" style="white-space:normal; text-decoration: none">al-Jamāhīrīyah al-‘Arabīyah al-Lībīyah ash-Sha‘bīyah al-Ishtirākīyah</span>)</i>, a "state of the masses" conceptualized by Gaddafi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987105Kawczynski201126.E2.80.9327Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168_141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987105Kawczynski201126.E2.80.9327Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168-141"><span>[</span>139<span>]</span></a></sup> Officially, the <i>Jamahiriya</i> was a <a href="/wiki/Direct_democracy" title="Direct democracy">direct democracy</a> in which the people ruled themselves through the 187 <a href="/wiki/Basic_People%27s_Congress_(political)" title="Basic People's Congress (political)">Basic People's Congresses</a>, where all adult Libyans participated and voted on national decisions. These then sent members to the annual General People's Congress, which was broadcast live on television. In principle, the People's Congresses were Libya's highest authority, with major decisions proposed by government officials or with Gaddafi himself requiring the consent of the People's Congresses.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168Vandewalle200819.E2.80.9320_142-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168Vandewalle200819.E2.80.9320-142"><span>[</span>140<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Libya_(1977-2011).svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg/200px-Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg.png" width="200" height="100" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg/300px-Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg/400px-Flag_of_Libya_%281977-2011%29.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="1000" data-file-height="500" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Libya_(1977-2011).svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Flag_of_Libya" title="Flag of Libya">Flag</a> of the Great Socialist People's Libyan Arab Jamahiriya.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Debate remained limited, and major decisions regarding the economy and defence were avoided or dealt with cursorily; the GPC largely remained "a rubber stamp" for Gaddafi's policies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729_143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729-143"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> On rare occasions, the GPC opposed Gaddafi's suggestions, sometimes successfully; notably, when Gaddafi called on primary schools to be abolished, believing that <a href="/wiki/Home_schooling" title="Home schooling" class="mw-redirect">home schooling</a> was healthier for children, the GPC rejected the idea.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729_143-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198729-143"><span>[</span>141<span>]</span></a></sup> In other instances, Gaddafi pushed through laws without the GPC's support, such as when he desired to allow women into the armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198667.E2.80.9368_144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198667.E2.80.9368-144"><span>[</span>142<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi proclaimed that the People's Congresses provided for Libya's every political need, rendering other political organizations unnecessary; all non-authorized groups, including political parties, professional associations, independent trade unions and women's groups, were banned.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201127Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168_145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201127Bruce_St._John2012166.E2.80.93168-145"><span>[</span>143<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>With preceding legal institutions abolished, Gaddafi envisioned the <i>Jamahiriya</i> as following the <a href="/wiki/Qur%27an" title="Qur'an" class="mw-redirect">Qur'an</a> for legal guidance, adopting <i>sharia</i> law; he proclaimed "man-made" laws unnatural and dictatorial, only permitting <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">Allah</a>'s law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201127.E2.80.9328Bruce_St._John2012167_146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201127.E2.80.9328Bruce_St._John2012167-146"><span>[</span>144<span>]</span></a></sup> Within a year he was backtracking, announcing that <i>sharia</i> was inappropriate for the <i>Jamahiriya</i> because it guaranteed the protection of private property, contravening <i>The Green Book'</i>s socialism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200828_147-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200828-147"><span>[</span>145<span>]</span></a></sup> His emphasis on placing his own work on a par with the Qur'an led conservative clerics to accuse him of <i><a href="/wiki/Shirk_(Islam)" title="Shirk (Islam)">shirk</a></i>, furthering their opposition to his regime.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198650_112-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198650-112"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> In July, a <a href="/wiki/Libyan%E2%80%93Egyptian_War" title="Libyan–Egyptian War">border war broke out</a> with Egypt, in which the Egyptians defeated Libya despite their technological inferiority. The conflict lasted one week before both sides agreed to sign a peace treaty that was brokered by several Arab states.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987105Vandewalle200835Kawczynski201167.E2.80.9368Bruce_St._John2012183_148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987105Vandewalle200835Kawczynski201167.E2.80.9368Bruce_St._John2012183-148"><span>[</span>146<span>]</span></a></sup> That year, Gaddafi was invited to <a href="/wiki/Moscow" title="Moscow">Moscow</a> by the Soviet government in recognition of their increasing commercial relationship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012180_149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012180-149"><span>[</span>147<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Revolutionary_Committees_and_furthering_socialism:_1978.E2.80.9380">Revolutionary Committees and furthering socialism: 1978–80</span></h3>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:246px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #ACE1AF;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"If socialism is defined as a redistribution of wealth and resources, a socialist revolution clearly occurred in Libya after 1969 and most especially in the second half of the 1970s. The management of the economy was increasingly socialist in intent and effect with wealth in housing, capital and land significantly redistributed or in the process of redistribution. Private enterprise was virtually eliminated, largely replaced by a centrally controlled economy."</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: right;"><cite>— Libyan Studies scholar Ronald St Bruce.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012173_150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012173-150"><span>[</span>148<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>In December 1978, Gaddafi stepped down as Secretary-General of the GPC, announcing his new focus on revolutionary rather than governmental activities; this was part of his new emphasis on separating the apparatus of the revolution from the government. Although no longer in a formal governmental post, he adopted the title of "Leader of the Revolution" and continued as commander-in-chief of the armed forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200826Kawczynski20113Bruce_St._John2012169_151-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200826Kawczynski20113Bruce_St._John2012169-151"><span>[</span>149<span>]</span></a></sup> He continued exerting considerable influence over Libya, with many critics insisting that the structure of Libya's direct democracy gave him "the freedom to manipulate outcomes".<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_robbins_152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_robbins-152"><span>[</span>150<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Libya began to turn towards socialism. In March 1978, the government issued guidelines for housing redistribution, attempting to ensure the population that every adult Libyan owned his own home and that nobody was enslaved to paying their rent. Most families were banned from owning more than one house, while former rental properties were seized and sold to the tenants at a heavily subsidized price.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987111Kawczynski2011221Bruce_St._John2012171.E2.80.93172_153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987111Kawczynski2011221Bruce_St._John2012171.E2.80.93172-153"><span>[</span>151<span>]</span></a></sup> In September, Gaddafi called for the People's Committees to eliminate the "bureaucracy of the public sector" and the "dictatorship of the private sector"; the People's Committees took control of several hundred companies, converting them into <a href="/wiki/Worker_cooperative" title="Worker cooperative">worker cooperatives</a> run by elected representatives.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987110.E2.80.93111Bruce_St._John2012168_154-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987110.E2.80.93111Bruce_St._John2012168-154"><span>[</span>152<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On 2 March 1979, the GPC announced the separation of government and revolution, the latter being represented by new Revolutionary Committees, who operated in tandem with the People's Committees in schools, universities, unions, the police force and the military.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171_155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171-155"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Dominated by revolutionary zealots, the Revolutionary Committees were led by <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mohammad_Maghgoub&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Mohammad Maghgoub (page does not exist)">Mohammad Maghgoub</a> and a Central Coordinating Office, and met with Gaddafi annually.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171_155-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171-155"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> Publishing a weekly magazine <i>The Green March</i> (<i>al-Zahf al-Akhdar</i>), in October 1980 they took control of the press. Responsible for perpetuating revolutionary fervour, they performed ideological surveillance, later adopting a significant security role, making arrests and putting people on trial according to the "law of the revolution" (<i>qanun al-thawra</i>).<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171_155-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987116.E2.80.93117.2C_127Vandewalle200825.E2.80.9326Kawczynski201131Bruce_St._John2012169.E2.80.93171-155"><span>[</span>153<span>]</span></a></sup> With no legal code or safeguards, the administration of revolutionary justice was largely arbitrary and resulted in widespread abuses and the suppression of <a href="/wiki/Civil_liberties" title="Civil liberties">civil liberties</a>: the "Green Terror."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987117Vandewalle200828Bruce_St._John2012174_156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987117Vandewalle200828Bruce_St._John2012174-156"><span>[</span>154<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1979, the committees began the redistribution of land in the Jefara plain, continuing through 1981.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012172_157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012172-157"><span>[</span>155<span>]</span></a></sup> In May 1980, measures to redistribute and equalize wealth were implemented; anyone with over 1000 <a href="/wiki/Dinar" title="Dinar">dinar</a> in his bank account saw that extra money expropriated.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987128Kawczynski2011221Bruce_St._John2012172_158-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987128Kawczynski2011221Bruce_St._John2012172-158"><span>[</span>156<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year, the GPC announced that the government would take control of all import, export and distribution functions, with state supermarkets replacing privately owned businesses; this led to a decline in the availability of consumer goods and the development of a thriving <a href="/wiki/Black_market" title="Black market">black market</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198728Vandewalle200821Kawczynski2011220Bruce_St._John2012172_159-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198728Vandewalle200821Kawczynski2011220Bruce_St._John2012172-159"><span>[</span>157<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: left; clear: left; margin: 0.5em 1.4em 0.8em 0; width:246px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #ACE1AF;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"I have created a Utopia here in Libya. Not an imaginary one that people write about in books, but a concrete Utopia."</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: right;"><cite>— Muammar Gaddafi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198726_160-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198726-160"><span>[</span>158<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>The <i>Jamahiriya'</i>s radical direction earned the government many enemies. In February 1978, Gaddafi discovered that his head of military intelligence was plotting to kill him, and began to increasingly entrust security to his Qaddadfa tribe.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987127.E2.80.93128Vandewalle200819_161-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987127.E2.80.93128Vandewalle200819-161"><span>[</span>159<span>]</span></a></sup> Many who had seen their wealth and property confiscated turned against the administration, and a number of western-funded opposition groups were founded by exiles. Most prominent was the <a href="/wiki/National_Front_for_the_Salvation_of_Libya" title="National Front for the Salvation of Libya">National Front for the Salvation of Libya</a> (NFSL), founded in 1981 by <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Magariaf" title="Mohammed Magariaf">Mohammed Magariaf</a>, which orchestrated militant attacks against Libya's government,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198679Vandewalle200832Bruce_St._John2012173.E2.80.93174_162-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198679Vandewalle200832Bruce_St._John2012173.E2.80.93174-162"><span>[</span>160<span>]</span></a></sup> while another, al-Borkan, began killing Libyan diplomats abroad.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198679BlundyLycett1987156_163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198679BlundyLycett1987156-163"><span>[</span>161<span>]</span></a></sup> Following Gaddafi's command to kill these "stray dogs", under Colonel <a href="/w/index.php?title=Younis_Bilgasim&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Younis Bilgasim (page does not exist)">Younis Bilgasim</a>'s leadership, the Revolutionary Committees set up overseas branches to suppress counter-revolutionary activity, assassinating various dissidents.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987133.E2.80.93137Vandewalle200827Bruce_St._John2012171_164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987133.E2.80.93137Vandewalle200827Bruce_St._John2012171-164"><span>[</span>162<span>]</span></a></sup> Although nearby nations like Syria also used hit squads, Gaddafi was unusual in publicly bragging about his administration's use of them; in June 1980, he ordered all dissidents to return home or be "liquidated wherever you are."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987138_165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987138-165"><span>[</span>163<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1979, the U.S. placed Libya on its list of <a href="/wiki/State_sponsors_of_terrorism" title="State sponsors of terrorism" class="mw-redirect">state sponsors of terrorism</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012179_166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012179-166"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> while at the end of the year <a href="/wiki/1979_U.S._embassy_burning_in_Libya" title="1979 U.S. embassy burning in Libya">a demonstration torched the U.S. embassy</a> in Tripoli in solidarity with the perpetrators of the <a href="/wiki/Iran_hostage_crisis" title="Iran hostage crisis">Iran hostage crisis</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987197.E2.80.93198Kawczynski2011115Bruce_St._John2012179_167-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987197.E2.80.93198Kawczynski2011115Bruce_St._John2012179-167"><span>[</span>165<span>]</span></a></sup> The following year, Libyan fighters began intercepting U.S. fighter jets flying over the Mediterranean, signalling the collapse of relations between the two countries.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012179_166-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012179-166"><span>[</span>164<span>]</span></a></sup> Libyan relations with Lebanon and <a href="/wiki/Shi%27ite" title="Shi'ite" class="mw-redirect">Shi'ite</a> communities across the world also deteriorated due to the August 1978 disappearance of imam <a href="/wiki/Musa_al-Sadr" title="Musa al-Sadr">Musa al-Sadr</a> when visiting Libya; the Lebanese accused Gaddafi of having him killed or imprisoned, a charge he denied.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987157.E2.80.93158Kawczynski201170.E2.80.9371Bruce_St._John2012239_168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987157.E2.80.93158Kawczynski201170.E2.80.9371Bruce_St._John2012239-168"><span>[</span>166<span>]</span></a></sup> Relations with Syria improved, as Gaddafi and Syrian President <a href="/wiki/Hafez_al-Assad" title="Hafez al-Assad">Hafez al-Assad</a> shared an enmity with Israel and Egypt's Sadat. In 1980, they proposed a political union, with Libya paying off Syria's £1 billion debt to the Soviet Union; although pressures led Assad to pull out, they remained allies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201168.E2.80.9369_169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski201168.E2.80.9369-169"><span>[</span>167<span>]</span></a></sup> Another key ally was Uganda, and in 1979, Gaddafi sent 2,500 troops into <a href="/wiki/Uganda-Tanzania_War" title="Uganda-Tanzania War" class="mw-redirect">Uganda to defend the regime</a> of President <a href="/wiki/Idi_Amin" title="Idi Amin">Idi Amin</a> from Tanzanian invaders. The mission failed; 400 Libyans were killed and they were forced to retreat.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987185.E2.80.93186Kawczynski201178.E2.80.9379Bruce_St._John2012189_170-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987185.E2.80.93186Kawczynski201178.E2.80.9379Bruce_St._John2012189-170"><span>[</span>168<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi later came to regret his alliance with Amin, openly criticising him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986105_171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986105-171"><span>[</span>169<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflict_with_the_US_and_its_allies:_1981.E2.80.9386">Conflict with the US and its allies: 1981–86</span></h3>
<p>The early and mid-1980s saw economic trouble for Libya; from 1982 to 1986, the country's annual oil revenues dropped from $21 billion to $5.4 billion.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198731Vandewalle200823Kawczynski2011104Bruce_St._John2012192_172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198731Vandewalle200823Kawczynski2011104Bruce_St._John2012192-172"><span>[</span>170<span>]</span></a></sup> Focusing on irrigation projects, 1983 saw construction start on "Gaddafi's Pet Project", the <a href="/wiki/Great_Manmade_River" title="Great Manmade River" class="mw-redirect">Great Manmade River</a>; although designed to be finished by the end of the decade, it remained incomplete at the start of the 21st century.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986119Kawczynski2011224Bruce_St._John2012249_173-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986119Kawczynski2011224Bruce_St._John2012249-173"><span>[</span>171<span>]</span></a></sup> Military spending increased, while other administrative budgets were cut back.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986116Vandewalle200835_174-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986116Vandewalle200835-174"><span>[</span>172<span>]</span></a></sup> Libya had long supported the <a href="/wiki/Frolinat" title="Frolinat" class="mw-redirect">Frolinat</a> militia in neighbouring Chad, and in December 1980, re-invaded Chad at the request of the Frolinat-controlled <a href="/wiki/Transitional_Government_of_National_Unity" title="Transitional Government of National Unity">GUNT government</a> to aid in the civil war; in January 1981, Gaddafi suggested a political merger. The <a href="/wiki/Organisation_of_African_Unity" title="Organisation of African Unity">Organisation of African Unity</a> (OAU) rejected this, and called for a Libyan withdrawal, which came about in November 1981. The civil war resumed, and so Libya sent troops back in, clashing with French forces who supported the southern Chadian forces.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987187.E2.80.93190Vandewalle200835Bruce_St._John2012189.E2.80.93190_175-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987187.E2.80.93190Vandewalle200835Bruce_St._John2012189.E2.80.93190-175"><span>[</span>173<span>]</span></a></sup> Many African nations had tired of Libya's policies of interference in foreign affairs; by 1980, nine African states had cut off diplomatic relations with Libya,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012189_176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012189-176"><span>[</span>174<span>]</span></a></sup> while in 1982 the OAU cancelled its scheduled conference in Tripoli in order to prevent Gaddafi gaining chairmanship.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986103Kawczynski201181Bruce_St._John2012190.E2.80.93191_177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986103Kawczynski201181Bruce_St._John2012190.E2.80.93191-177"><span>[</span>175<span>]</span></a></sup> Proposing political unity with Morocco, in August 1984, Gaddafi and Moroccan monarch <a href="/wiki/Hassan_II_of_Morocco" title="Hassan II of Morocco">Hassan II</a> signed the Oujda Treaty, forming the Arab-African Union; such a union was considered surprising due to the strong political differences and longstanding enmity that existed between the two governments. Relations remained strained, particularly due to Morocco's friendly relations with the U.S. and Israel; in August 1986, Hassan abolished the union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987214Kawczynski201172.E2.80.9375Bruce_St._John2012216_178-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987214Kawczynski201172.E2.80.9375Bruce_St._John2012216-178"><span>[</span>176<span>]</span></a></sup> Domestic threats continued to plague Gaddafi; in May 1984, his <a href="/wiki/Bab_al-Azizia" title="Bab al-Azizia">Bab al-Azizia</a> home was unsuccessfully attacked by a joint NFSL-Muslim Brotherhood militia, and in the aftermath 5000 dissidents were arrested.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198670BlundyLycett1987178_179-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198670BlundyLycett1987178-179"><span>[</span>177<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg/220px-13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg" width="220" height="178" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg/330px-13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2c/13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg/440px-13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg 2x" data-file-width="500" data-file-height="404" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:13th_anniversary_of_the_Revolution.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
13th Anniversary of the 1 September Revolution on postage stamp, Libya 1982.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>In 1981, the new US President <a href="/wiki/Ronald_Reagan" title="Ronald Reagan">Ronald Reagan</a> pursued a hard line approach to Libya, erroneously considering it a <a href="/wiki/Puppet_regime" title="Puppet regime" class="mw-redirect">puppet regime</a> of the Soviet Union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011115.E2.80.93116.2C_120Bruce_St._John2012179.E2.80.93180_180-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011115.E2.80.93116.2C_120Bruce_St._John2012179.E2.80.93180-180"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> In turn, Gaddafi played up his commercial relationship with the Soviets, visiting Moscow again in April 1981 and 1985, and threatening to join the <a href="/wiki/Warsaw_Pact" title="Warsaw Pact">Warsaw Pact</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198698.E2.80.9399Kawczynski2011115Bruce_St._John2012210.E2.80.93211_181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198698.E2.80.9399Kawczynski2011115Bruce_St._John2012210.E2.80.93211-181"><span>[</span>179<span>]</span></a></sup> The Soviets were nevertheless cautious of Gaddafi, seeing him as an unpredictable extremist.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198697BlundyLycett1987183_182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198697BlundyLycett1987183-182"><span>[</span>180<span>]</span></a></sup> Beginning military exercises in the Gulf of Sirte – an area of sea that Libya claimed as a part of its territorial waters – <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra_incident_(1981)" title="Gulf of Sidra incident (1981)">in August 1981 the U.S. shot down</a> two Libyan <a href="/wiki/Sukhoi_Su-17" title="Sukhoi Su-17">Su-22</a> planes monitoring them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200836Kawczynski2011118.E2.80.93119_183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200836Kawczynski2011118.E2.80.93119-183"><span>[</span>181<span>]</span></a></sup> Closing down Libya's embassy in <a href="/wiki/Washington,_D.C." title="Washington, D.C.">Washington D.C.</a>, Reagan advised U.S. companies operating in the country to reduce the number of American personnel stationed there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200837Kawczynski2011117.E2.80.93118Bruce_St._John2012180_184-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200837Kawczynski2011117.E2.80.93118Bruce_St._John2012180-184"><span>[</span>182<span>]</span></a></sup> In March 1982, the U.S. implemented an embargo of Libyan oil,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987207.E2.80.93208Vandewalle200837Kawczynski2011117-18Bruce_St._John2012181_185-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987207.E2.80.93208Vandewalle200837Kawczynski2011117-18Bruce_St._John2012181-185"><span>[</span>183<span>]</span></a></sup> and in January 1986 ordered all U.S. companies to cease operating in the country, although several hundred workers remained.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198727.2C_208Kawczynski2011117.E2.80.93118Bruce_St._John2012176_186-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198727.2C_208Kawczynski2011117.E2.80.93118Bruce_St._John2012176-186"><span>[</span>184<span>]</span></a></sup> Diplomatic relations also broke down with the U.K., after Libyan diplomats were accused in the shooting death <a href="/wiki/Yvonne_Fletcher" title="Yvonne Fletcher" class="mw-redirect">Yvonne Fletcher</a>, a British policewoman stationed outside their London embassy, in April 1984.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987175.E2.80.93178Vandewalle200837Bruce_St._John2012209_187-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987175.E2.80.93178Vandewalle200837Bruce_St._John2012209-187"><span>[</span>185<span>]</span></a></sup> In Spring 1986, <a href="/wiki/Action_in_the_Gulf_of_Sidra_(1986)" title="Action in the Gulf of Sidra (1986)">the U.S. Navy again began performing exercises in the Gulf of Sirte</a>; the Libyan military retaliated, but failed as the U.S. sank several Libyan ships.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011121.E2.80.93122_188-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011121.E2.80.93122-188"><span>[</span>186<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>After the U.S. accused Libya of orchestrating the <a href="/wiki/1986_Berlin_discotheque_bombing" title="1986 Berlin discotheque bombing">1986 Berlin discotheque bombing</a>, in which two American soldiers died, Reagan decided to retaliate militarily.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19874.E2.80.935Kawczynski2011122_189-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19874.E2.80.935Kawczynski2011122-189"><span>[</span>187<span>]</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> were critical of the move, believing that Syria were a greater threat and that an attack would strengthen Gaddafi's reputation; however Libya was recognised as a "soft target."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19875.E2.80.936_190-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19875.E2.80.936-190"><span>[</span>188<span>]</span></a></sup> Reagan was supported by the U.K. but opposed by other European allies, who argued that it would contravene international law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris1986102Kawczynski2011123.E2.80.93125_191-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris1986102Kawczynski2011123.E2.80.93125-191"><span>[</span>189<span>]</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/1986_United_States_bombing_of_Libya" title="1986 United States bombing of Libya">Operation El Dorado Canyon</a>, orchestrated on 15 April 1986, U.S. military planes launched a series of air-strikes on Libya, bombing military installations in various parts of the country, killing around 100 Libyans, including several civilians. One of the targets had been Gaddafi's home. Himself unharmed, two of Gaddafi's sons were injured, and he claimed that his four-year-old adopted daughter Hanna was killed, although her existence has since been questioned. <sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19872.E2.80.933.2C_7.E2.80.9312Vandewalle200837Kawczynski2011127.E2.80.93129_192-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19872.E2.80.933.2C_7.E2.80.9312Vandewalle200837Kawczynski2011127.E2.80.93129-192"><span>[</span>190<span>]</span></a></sup> In the immediate aftermath, Gaddafi retreated to the desert to meditate,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198713.2C_210Kawczynski2011130_193-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198713.2C_210Kawczynski2011130-193"><span>[</span>191<span>]</span></a></sup> while there were sporadic clashes between Gaddafists and army officers who wanted to overthrow the government.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198712_194-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198712-194"><span>[</span>192<span>]</span></a></sup> Although the U.S. was condemned internationally, Reagan received a popularity boost at home.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011130_195-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011130-195"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> Publicly lambasting U.S. imperialism, Gaddafi's reputation as an anti-imperialist was strengthened both domestically and across the Arab world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715Bruce_St._John2012196_196-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715Bruce_St._John2012196-196"><span>[</span>194<span>]</span></a></sup> and in June 1986, he ordered the names of the month to be changed in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198730_197-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198730-197"><span>[</span>195<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Revolution_within_a_Revolution.22:_1987.E2.80.9398">"Revolution within a Revolution": 1987–98</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg/220px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg" width="220" height="331" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg/330px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/36/Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg/440px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2832" data-file-height="4256" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_al-Gaddafi_at_the_AU_summit.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi at the twelfth African Union cofnerence in 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>The late 1980s saw a series of liberalising economic reforms within Libya designed to cope with the decline in oil revenues. In May 1987, Gaddafi announced the start of the "Revolution within a Revolution", which began with reforms to industry and agriculture and saw the re-opening of small business.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011225Bruce_St._John2012194_198-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011225Bruce_St._John2012194-198"><span>[</span>196<span>]</span></a></sup> Restrictions were placed on the activities of the Revolutionary Committees; in March 1988, their role was narrowed by the newly created Ministry for Mass Mobilization and Revolutionary Leadership to restrict their violence and judicial role, while in August 1988 Gaddafi publicly criticised them,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200829Bruce_St._John2012194.E2.80.93195.2C_199.E2.80.93200_199-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200829Bruce_St._John2012194.E2.80.93195.2C_199.E2.80.93200-199"><span>[</span>197<span>]</span></a></sup> asserting that "they deviated, harmed, tortured" and that "the true revolutionary does not practise repression."<sup id="cite_ref-ham_40_1_200-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ham_40_1-200"><span>[</span>198<span>]</span></a></sup> In March, hundreds of political prisoners were freed, with Gaddafi falsely claiming that there were no further political prisoners in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200845Bruce_St._John2012222_201-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200845Bruce_St._John2012222-201"><span>[</span>199<span>]</span></a></sup> In June, Libya's government issued the Great Green Charter on Human Rights in the Era of the Masses, in which 27 articles laid out goals, rights and guarantees to improve the situation of human rights in Libya, restricting the use of the <a href="/wiki/Death_penalty" title="Death penalty" class="mw-redirect">death penalty</a> and calling for its eventual abolition. Many of the measures suggested in the charter would be implemented the following year, although others remained inactive.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200845.E2.80.9346Bruce_St._John2012197.E2.80.93198_202-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200845.E2.80.9346Bruce_St._John2012197.E2.80.93198-202"><span>[</span>200<span>]</span></a></sup> Also in 1989, the government founded the <a href="/wiki/Al-Gaddafi_International_Prize_for_Human_Rights" title="Al-Gaddafi International Prize for Human Rights">Al-Gaddafi International Prize for Human Rights</a>, to be awarded to figures from the Third World who had struggled against colonialism and imperialism; the first year's winner was South African anti-apartheid activist <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012199_203-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012199-203"><span>[</span>201<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1994 through to 1997, the government initiated cleansing committees to root out corruption, particularly in the economic sector.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012197.E2.80.93198_204-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012197.E2.80.93198-204"><span>[</span>202<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the aftermath of the 1986 U.S. attack, the army was purged of perceived disloyal elements,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011130_195-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011130-195"><span>[</span>193<span>]</span></a></sup> and in 1988, Gaddafi announced the creation of a popular militia to replace the army and police.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200838Bruce_St._John2012200_205-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200838Bruce_St._John2012200-205"><span>[</span>203<span>]</span></a></sup> In 1987, <a href="/wiki/Libya_and_weapons_of_mass_destruction" title="Libya and weapons of mass destruction">Libya began production</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mustard_gas" title="Mustard gas" class="mw-redirect">mustard gas</a> at a facility in Rabta, although publicly denying it was stockpiling chemical weapons,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012201.E2.80.93204_206-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012201.E2.80.93204-206"><span>[</span>204<span>]</span></a></sup> and unsuccessfully attempted to develop nuclear weapons.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011180.E2.80.93181_207-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011180.E2.80.93181-207"><span>[</span>205<span>]</span></a></sup> The period also saw a growth in domestic <a href="/wiki/Islamism" title="Islamism">Islamist</a> opposition, formulated into groups like the <a href="/wiki/Muslim_Brotherhood" title="Muslim Brotherhood">Muslim Brotherhood</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Islamic_Fighting_Group" title="Libyan Islamic Fighting Group">Libyan Islamic Fighting Group</a>. A number of assassination attempts against Gaddafi were foiled, and in turn, 1989 saw the security forces raid mosques believed to be centres of counter-revolutionary preaching.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011166.E2.80.93167.2C_236Bruce_St._John2012221.E2.80.93222_208-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011166.E2.80.93167.2C_236Bruce_St._John2012221.E2.80.93222-208"><span>[</span>206<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 1993, elements of the increasingly marginalised army initiated a failed coup in <a href="/wiki/Misrata" title="Misrata">Misrata</a>, while in September 1995, Islamists launched an insurgency in Benghazi, and in July 1996 an anti-Gaddafist football riot broke out in Tripoli.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011166Bruce_St._John2012223_209-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011166Bruce_St._John2012223-209"><span>[</span>207<span>]</span></a></sup> The Revolutionary Committees experienced a resurgence to combat these Islamists.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200829_210-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200829-210"><span>[</span>208<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In 1989, Gaddafi was overjoyed by the foundation of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Maghreb_Union" title="Arab Maghreb Union">Arab Maghreb Union</a>, uniting Libya in an economic pact with Mauritania, Morocco, Tunisia and Algeria, viewing it as beginnings of a new Pan-Arab union.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011188Bruce_St._John2012216.E2.80.93218_211-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011188Bruce_St._John2012216.E2.80.93218-211"><span>[</span>209<span>]</span></a></sup> Meanwhile, Libya stepped up its support for anti-western militants such as the Provisional IRA,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012197_212-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012197-212"><span>[</span>210<span>]</span></a></sup> and in 1988, <a href="/wiki/Pan_Am_Flight_103" title="Pan Am Flight 103">Pan Am Flight 103</a> was blown up over <a href="/wiki/Lockerbie" title="Lockerbie">Lockerbie</a> in <a href="/wiki/Scotland" title="Scotland">Scotland</a>, killing 243 passengers and 16 crew members, plus 11 people on the ground. British police investigations identified two Libyans – <a href="/wiki/Abdelbaset_al-Megrahi" title="Abdelbaset al-Megrahi">Abdelbaset al-Megrahi</a> and <a href="/wiki/Lamin_Khalifah_Fhimah" title="Lamin Khalifah Fhimah">Lamin Khalifah Fhimah</a> – as the chief suspects, and in November 1991 issued a declaration demanding that Libya hand them over. When Gaddafi refused, citing the <a href="/wiki/Montreal_Convention" title="Montreal Convention">Montreal Convention</a>, the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a> (UN) imposed Resolution 748 in March 1992, initiating economic sanctions against Libya which had deep repercussions for the country's economy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200839Kawczynski2011133.E2.80.93140Bruce_St._John2012205.E2.80.93207_213-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200839Kawczynski2011133.E2.80.93140Bruce_St._John2012205.E2.80.93207-213"><span>[</span>211<span>]</span></a></sup> The country suffered an estimated $900 million financial loss as a result.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200842_214-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle200842-214"><span>[</span>212<span>]</span></a></sup> Further problems arose with the west when in January 1989, <a href="/wiki/Gulf_of_Sidra_incident_(1989)" title="Gulf of Sidra incident (1989)">two Libyan warplanes were shot down by the U.S. off the Libyan coast</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012202_215-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012202-215"><span>[</span>213<span>]</span></a></sup> Many African states opposed the UN sanctions, with Mandela criticising them on a visit to Gaddafi in October 1997, when he praised Libya for its work in fighting apartheid and awarded Gaddafi the <a href="/wiki/Order_of_Good_Hope" title="Order of Good Hope">Order of Good Hope</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011147Bruce_St._John2012205.E2.80.93206_216-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011147Bruce_St._John2012205.E2.80.93206-216"><span>[</span>214<span>]</span></a></sup> They would only be suspended in 1998 when Libya agreed to allow the extradition of the suspects to the <a href="/wiki/Scottish_Court_in_the_Netherlands" title="Scottish Court in the Netherlands">Scottish Court in the Netherlands</a>, in a process overseen by Mandela.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011146.E2.80.93148Bruce_St._John2012206_217-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011146.E2.80.93148Bruce_St._John2012206-217"><span>[</span>215<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pan-Africanism.2C_reconciliation_and_privatization:_1999.E2.80.932011">Pan-Africanism, reconciliation and privatization: 1999–2011</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg/170px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg" width="170" height="240" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg/255px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg/340px-Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1656" data-file-height="2337" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_al-Gaddafi-2-30112006.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Muammar Gaddafi wearing an insignie showing the image of the African continent</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>As the 20th century came to a close, Gaddafi increasingly rejected Arab nationalism, frustrated by the failure of his Pan-Arab ideals; instead he turned to <a href="/wiki/Pan-Africanism" title="Pan-Africanism">Pan-Africanism</a>, emphasising Libya's African identity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011142Bruce_St._John2012227_218-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011142Bruce_St._John2012227-218"><span>[</span>216<span>]</span></a></sup> From 1997 to 2000, Libya initiated cooperative agreements or bilateral aid arrangements with 10 African states,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012229_219-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012229-219"><span>[</span>217<span>]</span></a></sup> and in 1999 joined the <a href="/wiki/Community_of_Sahel-Saharan_States" title="Community of Sahel-Saharan States">Community of Sahel-Saharan States</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011189_220-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011189-220"><span>[</span>218<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 1999, Gaddafi visited Mandela in South Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012226_221-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012226-221"><span>[</span>219<span>]</span></a></sup> and the following month attended the OAU summit in Algiers, calling for greater political and economic integration across the continent and advocating the foundation of a <a href="/wiki/United_States_of_Africa" title="United States of Africa">United States of Africa</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012227.E2.80.93228_222-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012227.E2.80.93228-222"><span>[</span>220<span>]</span></a></sup> He became one of the founders of the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> (AU), initiated in July 2002 to replace the OAU; at the opening ceremonies, he proclaimed that African states should reject conditional aid from the developed world, a direct contrast to the message of South African President <a href="/wiki/Thabo_Mbeki" title="Thabo Mbeki">Thabo Mbeki</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190Bruce_St._John2012229_223-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190Bruce_St._John2012229-223"><span>[</span>221<span>]</span></a></sup> At the third AU summit, held in Libya in July 2005, he called for a greater level of integration, advocating a single AU passport, a common defence system and a single currency, utilising the slogan: "The United States of Africa is the hope."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190.E2.80.93191Bruce_St._John2012230_224-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190.E2.80.93191Bruce_St._John2012230-224"><span>[</span>222<span>]</span></a></sup> In June 2005, Libya joined the <a href="/wiki/Common_Market_for_Eastern_and_Southern_Africa" title="Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa">Common Market for Eastern and Southern Africa</a> (COMESA),<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012231_225-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012231-225"><span>[</span>223<span>]</span></a></sup> and in August 2008 Gaddafi was proclaimed "<a href="/wiki/King_of_Kings" title="King of Kings">King of Kings</a>" by an assembled committee of traditional African <a href="/wiki/Tribal_chief" title="Tribal chief">leaders</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011188Bruce_St._John2012270.E2.80.93271_226-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011188Bruce_St._John2012270.E2.80.93271-226"><span>[</span>224<span>]</span></a></sup> On 1&#160;February&#160;2009, his "<a href="/wiki/Coronation" title="Coronation">coronation</a> ceremony" was held in <a href="/wiki/Addis_Ababa" title="Addis Ababa">Addis Ababa</a>, Ethiopia, coinciding with Gaddafi's election as AU chairman for a year.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190Bruce_St._John2012272_227-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011190Bruce_St._John2012272-227"><span>[</span>225<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>The era saw Libya's return to the international arena. In 1999, Libya began secret talks with the British government to normalise relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011215_228-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011215-228"><span>[</span>226<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2001, Gaddafi condemned the <a href="/wiki/September_11_attacks" title="September 11 attacks">September 11 attacks</a> on the U.S. by <a href="/wiki/Al-Qaeda" title="Al-Qaeda">al-Qaeda</a>, expressing sympathy with the victims and calling for Libyan involvement in the <a href="/wiki/War_on_Terror" title="War on Terror">War on Terror</a> against militant Islamism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011220Kawczynski2011176Bruce_St._John2012243_229-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011220Kawczynski2011176Bruce_St._John2012243-229"><span>[</span>227<span>]</span></a></sup> His government continued suppressing domestic Islamism, at the same time as Gaddafi called for the wider application of <i>sharia</i> law.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012254_230-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012254-230"><span>[</span>228<span>]</span></a></sup> Libya also cemented connections with China and North Korea, being visited by Chinese President <a href="/wiki/Jiang_Zemin" title="Jiang Zemin">Jiang Zemin</a> in April 2002.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012235_231-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012235-231"><span>[</span>229<span>]</span></a></sup> Influenced by the events of the <a href="/wiki/Iraq_War" title="Iraq War">Iraq War</a>, in December 2003, Libya renounced its possession of <a href="/wiki/Weapon_of_mass_destruction" title="Weapon of mass destruction">weapons of mass destruction</a>, decommissioning its chemical and nuclear weapons programs.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011217Kawczynski2011162.2C_184Bruce_St._John2012244_232-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011217Kawczynski2011162.2C_184Bruce_St._John2012244-232"><span>[</span>230<span>]</span></a></sup> Relations with the U.S. improved as a result,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011178.E2.80.93179Bruce_St._John2012245_233-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011178.E2.80.93179Bruce_St._John2012245-233"><span>[</span>231<span>]</span></a></sup> while U.K. Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Tony_Blair" title="Tony Blair">Tony Blair</a> met with Gaddafi in the Libyan desert in March 2004.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011240.E2.80.93241Bruce_St._John2012240.E2.80.93241_234-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011240.E2.80.93241Bruce_St._John2012240.E2.80.93241-234"><span>[</span>232<span>]</span></a></sup> The following month, Gaddafi travelled to the headquarters of the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a> (EU) in <a href="/wiki/Brussels" title="Brussels">Brussels</a>, signifying improved relations between Libya and the EU, the latter ending its remaining sanctions in October.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011175Bruce_St._John2012237_235-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011175Bruce_St._John2012237-235"><span>[</span>233<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 2010, the EU paid Libya €50 million to stop African migrants passing into Europe; Gaddafi encouraged the move, saying that it was necessary to prevent the loss of European cultural identity to a new "Black Europe".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012274_236-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012274-236"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup> Removed from the U.S. list of state sponsors of terrorism in 2006,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011176_237-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011176-237"><span>[</span>235<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi nevertheless continued his anti-western rhetoric, and at the <a href="/wiki/Second_Africa-South_America_Summit" title="Second Africa-South America Summit">Second Africa-South America Summit</a> in Venezuela in September 2009, joined Venezuelan President <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> in calling for an "anti-imperialist" front across Africa and Latin America. Gaddafi proposed the establishment of a South Atlantic Treaty Organization to rival <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-238" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-238"><span>[</span>236<span>]</span></a></sup> That month he also addressed the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_General_Assembly" title="United Nations General Assembly">United Nations General Assembly</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_York_City" title="New York City">New York</a> for the first time, using it to condemn "western aggression".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012276_239-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012276-239"><span>[</span>237<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-240" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-240"><span>[</span>238<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-241" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-241"><span>[</span>239<span>]</span></a></sup> In Spring 2010, Gaddafi proclaimed <i>jihad</i> against <a href="/wiki/Switzerland" title="Switzerland">Switzerland</a> after Swiss police accused two of his family members of criminal activity in the country, resulting in the breakdown of bilateral relations.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012274_236-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012274-236"><span>[</span>234<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Libya's economy witnessed increasing privatization; although rejecting the socialist policies of nationalized industry advocated in <i>The Green Book</i>, government figures asserted that they were forging "people's socialism" rather than capitalism.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012250_242-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012250-242"><span>[</span>240<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi welcomed these reforms, calling for wide-scale privatization in a March 2003 speech.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011224_243-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011224-243"><span>[</span>241<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2003, the oil industry was largely sold to private corporations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012247_244-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012247-244"><span>[</span>242<span>]</span></a></sup> and by 2004, there was $40 billion of direct foreign investment in Libya, a sixfold rise over 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011180Bruce_St._John2012248_245-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011180Bruce_St._John2012248-245"><span>[</span>243<span>]</span></a></sup> Sectors of Libya's population reacted against these reforms with public demonstrations,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012248_246-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012248-246"><span>[</span>244<span>]</span></a></sup> and in March 2006, revolutionary hard-liners took control of the GPC cabinet; although scaling back the pace of the changes, they did not halt them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011228Bruce_St._John2012249.E2.80.93250_247-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011228Bruce_St._John2012249.E2.80.93250-247"><span>[</span>245<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2010, plans were announced that would have seen half the Libyan economy privatized over the following decade.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012263.E2.80.93264_248-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012263.E2.80.93264-248"><span>[</span>246<span>]</span></a></sup> While there was no accompanying political liberalization, with Gaddafi retaining predominant control,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011231_249-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011231-249"><span>[</span>247<span>]</span></a></sup> in March 2010, the government devolved further powers to the municipal councils.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012257_250-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012257-250"><span>[</span>248<span>]</span></a></sup> Rising numbers of reformist technocrats attained positions in the country's governance; best known was Gaddafi's son and heir apparent <a href="/wiki/Saif_al-Islam_Gaddafi" title="Saif al-Islam Gaddafi">Saif al-Islam Gaddafi</a>, who was openly critical of Libya's human rights record. He led a group who proposed the drafting of the new constitution, although it was never adopted, and in October 2009 was appointed to head the PSLC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011225Bruce_St._John2012249.E2.80.93269_251-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011225Bruce_St._John2012249.E2.80.93269-251"><span>[</span>249<span>]</span></a></sup> Involved in encouraging tourism, Saif founded several privately run media channels in 2008, but after criticising the government they were nationalised in 2009.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011216.2C_227.E2.80.93228_252-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011216.2C_227.E2.80.93228-252"><span>[</span>250<span>]</span></a></sup> In October 2010, Gaddafi apologized to African leaders on behalf of Arab nations for their involvement in the African <a href="/wiki/Arab_slave_trade" title="Arab slave trade">slave trade</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-253" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-253"><span>[</span>251<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Libyan_Civil_War">Libyan Civil War</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Muammar_Gaddafi%27s_response_to_the_2011_Libyan_Civil_War" title="Muammar Gaddafi's response to the 2011 Libyan Civil War">Muammar Gaddafi's response to the 2011 Libyan Civil War</a> and <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Civil_War_(2011)" title="Libyan Civil War (2011)">Libyan Civil War (2011)</a></div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Origins:_February.E2.80.93March_2011">Origins: February–March 2011</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:282px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg/280px-Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg" width="280" height="186" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg/420px-Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/80/Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg/560px-Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="4592" data-file-height="3056" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Protest_In_Dublin_Gaddafi_Is_A_Murderer.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
People protesting against Gaddafi in <a href="/wiki/Dublin" title="Dublin">Dublin</a>, <a href="/wiki/Ireland" title="Ireland">Ireland</a>, March 2011.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Following the start of the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Spring" title="Arab Spring">Arab Spring</a> in 2011, Gaddafi spoke out in favour of Tunisian President <a href="/wiki/Zine_El_Abidine_Ben_Ali" title="Zine El Abidine Ben Ali">Zine El Abidine Ben Ali</a>, then threatened by the <a href="/wiki/Tunisian_revolution" title="Tunisian revolution" class="mw-redirect">Tunisian revolution</a>. He suggested that Tunisia's people would be satisfied if Ben Ali introduced a <i>Jamahiriyah</i> system there.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012278_254-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012278-254"><span>[</span>252<span>]</span></a></sup> Fearing domestic protest, Libya's government implemented preventative measures, reducing food prices, purging the army leadership of potential defectors and releasing several Islamist prisoners.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012282.E2.80.93283_255-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012282.E2.80.93283-255"><span>[</span>253<span>]</span></a></sup> They proved ineffective, and on 17 February 2011, major protests broke out against Gaddafi's government. Unlike Tunisia or Egypt, Libya was largely religiously homogenous and had no strong Islamist movement, but there was widespread dissatisfaction with the corruption and entrenched systems of patronage, while unemployment had reached around 30%.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011231Bruce_St._John2012279.E2.80.93281_256-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011231Bruce_St._John2012279.E2.80.93281-256"><span>[</span>254<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Accusing the rebels of being "drugged" and linked to al-Qaeda, Gaddafi proclaimed that he would die a <a href="/wiki/Martyr" title="Martyr">martyr</a> rather than leave Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011242_257-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011242-257"><span>[</span>255<span>]</span></a></sup> As he announced that the rebels would be "hunted down street by street, house by house and wardrobe by wardrobe",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011242.E2.80.93243_258-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011242.E2.80.93243-258"><span>[</span>256<span>]</span></a></sup> the army opened fire on protests in Benghazi, killing hundreds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012283_259-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012283-259"><span>[</span>257<span>]</span></a></sup> Shocked at the government's response, a number of senior politicians resigned or defected to the protesters' side.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012284Vandewalle2011236_260-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012284Vandewalle2011236-260"><span>[</span>258<span>]</span></a></sup> The uprising spread quickly through Libya's less economically developed eastern half.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236_261-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236-261"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup> By February's end, eastern cities like Benghazi, Misrata, al-Bayda and Tobruk were controlled by rebels,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012284_262-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012284-262"><span>[</span>260<span>]</span></a></sup> and the Benghazi-based <a href="/wiki/National_Transitional_Council" title="National Transitional Council">National Transitional Council</a> (NTC) had been founded to represent them.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286Human_Rights_Watch201216_263-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286Human_Rights_Watch201216-263"><span>[</span>261<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the conflict's early months it appeared that Gaddafi's government – with its greater firepower – would be victorious.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236_261-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236-261"><span>[</span>259<span>]</span></a></sup> Both sides disregarded the <a href="/wiki/Laws_of_war" title="Laws of war" class="mw-redirect">laws of war</a>, committing human rights abuses, including arbitrary arrests, torture, extrajudicial executions and revenge attacks.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201217.E2.80.9318_264-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201217.E2.80.9318-264"><span>[</span>262<span>]</span></a></sup> On 26 February the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council" title="United Nations Security Council">United Nations Security Council</a> passed <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1970" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1970">Resolution 1970</a>, suspending Libya from the <a href="/wiki/UN_Human_Rights_Council" title="UN Human Rights Council" class="mw-redirect">UN Human Rights Council</a>, implementing sanctions and calling for an <a href="/wiki/International_Criminal_Court" title="International Criminal Court">International Criminal Court</a> (ICC) investigation into the killing of unarmed civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Bruce_St._John2012284Human_Rights_Watch201216_265-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Bruce_St._John2012284Human_Rights_Watch201216-265"><span>[</span>263<span>]</span></a></sup> In March, the Security Council declared a <a href="/wiki/United_Nations_Security_Council_Resolution_1973" title="United Nations Security Council Resolution 1973">no fly zone</a> to protect the civilian population from aerial bombardment, calling on foreign nations to enforce it;
