it also specifically prohibited foreign occupation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Human_Rights_Watch201216_266-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Human_Rights_Watch201216-266"><span>[</span>264<span>]</span></a></sup> Ignoring this, Qatar sent hundreds of troops to support the dissidents, and along with France and the United Arab Emirates provided the NTC with weaponry and training.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201216_267-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201216-267"><span>[</span>265<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="NATO_intervention:_March.E2.80.93August_2011">NATO intervention: March–August 2011</span></h3>
<p>A week after the implementation of the no-fly zone, <a href="/wiki/NATO" title="NATO">NATO</a> announced that it would be enforced.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Bruce_St._John2012284_268-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVandewalle2011236Bruce_St._John2012284-268"><span>[</span>266<span>]</span></a></sup> On 30 April a NATO airstrike killed <a href="/wiki/Saif_al-Arab_Gaddafi" title="Saif al-Arab Gaddafi">Gaddafi's sixth son</a> and three of his grandsons in Tripoli, though Gaddafi and his wife were unharmed. Western officials remained divided over whether Gaddafi was a legitimate military target under the U.N. Security Council resolution. <a href="/wiki/U.S._Secretary_of_Defense" title="U.S. Secretary of Defense" class="mw-redirect">U.S. Secretary of Defense</a> <a href="/wiki/Robert_Gates" title="Robert Gates">Robert Gates</a> said that NATO was "not targeting Gaddafi specifically" but that his command-and-control facilities were legitimate targets—including a facility inside his sprawling Tripoli compound that was hit with airstrikes on 25 April.<sup id="cite_ref-269" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-269"><span>[</span>267<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_(5764877039).jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg/220px-Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg/330px-Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9e/Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg/440px-Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_%285764877039%29.jpg 2x" data-file-width="800" data-file-height="600" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Les_femmes_de_Libye_d%C3%A9chir%C3%A9es_entre_le_r%C3%A9gime_et_les_rebelles_(5764877039).jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Pro-Gaddafi protests in Tripoli, May 2011</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>On 27 June, the ICC issued arrest warrants for Gaddafi, his son Saif al-Islam, and his brother-in-law <a href="/wiki/Abdullah_Senussi" title="Abdullah Senussi">Abdullah Senussi</a>, head of state security, for charges concerning crimes against humanity.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011257Bruce_St._John2012286_270-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011257Bruce_St._John2012286-270"><span>[</span>268<span>]</span></a></sup> Libyan officials rejected the ICC, claiming that it had "no legitimacy whatsoever" and highlighting that "all of its activities are directed at African leaders".<sup id="cite_ref-AP_ICC_271-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AP_ICC-271"><span>[</span>269<span>]</span></a></sup> That month, <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty International</a> published their findings, in which they asserted that many of the accusations of mass human rights abuses made against Gaddafist forces lacked credible evidence, and were instead fabrications of the rebel forces which had been readily adopted by the western media. Amnesty International did however still accuse Gaddafi forces of numerous war crimes.<sup id="cite_ref-AmnestyRape_272-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmnestyRape-272"><span>[</span>270<span>]</span></a></sup> On 15 July 2011, at a meeting in Istanbul, over 30 governments recognised the NTC as the legitimate government of Libya. Gaddafi responded to the announcement with a speech on Libyan national television, in which he called on supporters to "Trample on those recognitions, trample on them under your feet&#160;... They are worthless".<sup id="cite_ref-INDtncofficialgov_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INDtncofficialgov-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Now with NATO support in the form of air cover, the rebel militia pushed westward, defeating loyalist armies and securing control of the centre of the country.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012285_273-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012285-273"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaining the support of <a href="/wiki/Amazigh" title="Amazigh" class="mw-redirect">Amazigh</a> (<a href="/wiki/Berberism" title="Berberism">Berber</a>) communities of the <a href="/wiki/Nafusa_Mountains" title="Nafusa Mountains">Nafusa Mountains</a>, who had long been persecuted as non-Arabic speakers under Gaddafi, the NTC armies surrounded Gaddafi loyalists in several key areas of western Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012285_273-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012285-273"><span>[</span>271<span>]</span></a></sup> In August, the rebels seized <a href="/wiki/Zlitan" title="Zlitan" class="mw-redirect">Zlitan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tripoli_(2011)" title="Battle of Tripoli (2011)">Tripoli</a>, ending the last vestiges of Gaddafist power.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286_274-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286-274"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> On 25 August, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> recognised the NTC to be "the legitimate representative of the Libyan state", on which basis Libya would resume its membership in the League.<sup id="cite_ref-taipeitimmes20110826_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taipeitimmes20110826-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Capture_and_death:_September.E2.80.93October_2011">Capture and death: September–October 2011</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Death_of_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Death of Muammar Gaddafi">Death of Muammar Gaddafi</a></div>
<p>Only a few towns in western Libya—such as Bani Walid, Sebha and Sirte—remained Gaddafist strongholds.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286_274-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286-274"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> Retreating to Sirte after Tripoli's fall,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201220_275-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201220-275"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi announced his willingness to negotiate for a handover to a transitional government, a suggestion rejected by the NTC.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286_274-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBruce_St._John2012286-274"><span>[</span>272<span>]</span></a></sup> Surrounding himself with bodyguards,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201220_275-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201220-275"><span>[</span>273<span>]</span></a></sup> he continually moved residences to escape NTC shelling, devoting his days to prayer and reading the Qur'an.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201221.E2.80.9322_276-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201221.E2.80.9322-276"><span>[</span>274<span>]</span></a></sup> On 20 October, Gaddafi broke out of Sirte's District 2 in a joint civilian-military convoy, hoping to take refuge in the Jarref Valley.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201223_277-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201223-277"><span>[</span>275<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> At around 8.30am, NATO bombers attacked, destroying at least 14 vehicles and killing at least 53.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201224.E2.80.9325_279-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201224.E2.80.9325-279"><span>[</span>277<span>]</span></a></sup> The convoy scattered, and Gaddafi and those closest to him fled to a nearby villa, which was shelled by rebel militia from Misrata. Fleeing to a construction site, Gaddafi and his inner consort hid inside drainage pipes while his bodyguards battled the rebels; in the conflict, Gaddafi suffered head injuries from a grenade blast while defence minister <a href="/wiki/Abu-Bakr_Yunis_Jabr" title="Abu-Bakr Yunis Jabr">Abu-Bakr Yunis Jabr</a> was killed.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201226.E2.80.9327_280-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201226.E2.80.9327-280"><span>[</span>278<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-281" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-281"><span>[</span>279<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A Misratan militia took Gaddafi prisoner, beating him, causing serious injuries; the events were filmed on a mobile phone. A video appears to picture Gaddafi being poked or stabbed in the rear end "with some kind of stick or knife"<sup id="cite_ref-282" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-282"><span>[</span>280<span>]</span></a></sup> or possibly a bayonet.<sup id="cite_ref-Irish_Times_283-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Irish_Times-283"><span>[</span>281<span>]</span></a></sup> Pulled onto the front of a pick-up truck, he fell off as it drove away. His semi-naked, lifeless body was then placed into an ambulance and taken to Misrata; upon arrival, he was found to be dead.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201228.E2.80.9329_284-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201228.E2.80.9329-284"><span>[</span>282<span>]</span></a></sup> Official NTC accounts claimed that Gaddafi was caught in a cross-fire and died from his bullet wounds.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> Other eye-witness accounts claimed that rebels had fatally shot Gaddafi in the stomach;<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> a rebel identifying himself as Senad el-Sadik el-Ureybi later claimed responsibility.<sup id="cite_ref-285" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-285"><span>[</span>283<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi's son <a href="/wiki/Mutassim_Gaddafi" title="Mutassim Gaddafi">Mutassim</a>, who had also been among the convoy, was also captured, and found dead several hours later, most probably from an <a href="/wiki/Extrajudicial_killing" title="Extrajudicial killing">extrajudicial execution</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201232.E2.80.9333_286-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201232.E2.80.9333-286"><span>[</span>284<span>]</span></a></sup> Around 140 Gaddafi loyalists were rounded up from the convoy; tied up and abused, the corpses of 66 were found at the nearby Mahari Hotel, victims of extrajudicial execution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201234.E2.80.9340_287-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201234.E2.80.9340-287"><span>[</span>285<span>]</span></a></sup> Libya's chief forensic pathologist, Dr. Othman al-Zintani, carried out the autopsies of Gaddafi, his son and Jabr in the days following their deaths; although the pathologist initially told the press that Gaddafi had died from a gunshot wound to the head, the autopsy report was not made public.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201243_288-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201243-288"><span>[</span>286<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>On the afternoon of Gaddafi's death, NTC Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/Mahmoud_Jibril" title="Mahmoud Jibril">Mahmoud Jibril</a> publicly revealed the news.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC2011_278-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC2011-278"><span>[</span>276<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi's corpse was placed in the freezer of a local market alongside the corpses of Yunis Jabr and Mutassim; the bodies were publicly displayed for four days, with Libyans from all over the country coming to view them.<sup id="cite_ref-289" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-289"><span>[</span>287<span>]</span></a></sup> In response to international calls, on 24 October Jibril announced that a commission would investigate Gaddafi's death.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201244_290-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHuman_Rights_Watch201244-290"><span>[</span>288<span>]</span></a></sup> On 25 October, the NTC announced that Gaddafi had been buried at an unidentified location in the desert; <a href="/wiki/Al_Aan_TV" title="Al Aan TV">Al Aan TV</a> showed amateur video footage of the funeral.<sup id="cite_ref-291" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-291"><span>[</span>289<span>]</span></a></sup> Seeking vengeance for the killing, Gaddafist sympathisers fatally wounded one of those who had captured Gaddafi, Omran Shaaban, near Bani Walid in September 2012.<sup id="cite_ref-292" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-292"><span>[</span>290<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_and_public_life">Personal and public life</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Ideology">Ideology</span></h3>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Political_ideology_of_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Political ideology of Muammar Gaddafi" class="mw-redirect">Political ideology of Muammar Gaddafi</a></div>
<p>As a schoolboy, Gaddafi adopted the ideologies of <a href="/wiki/Arab_nationalism" title="Arab nationalism">Arab nationalism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Arab_socialism" title="Arab socialism">Arab socialism</a>, influenced in particular by <a href="/wiki/Nasserism" title="Nasserism">Nasserism</a>, the thought of Egyptian revolutionary and president <a href="/wiki/Gamal_Abdel_Nasser" title="Gamal Abdel Nasser">Gamal Abdel Nasser</a>, whom Gaddafi adopted as his hero.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198643BlundyLycett198718_293-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198643BlundyLycett198718-293"><span>[</span>291<span>]</span></a></sup> During the early 1970s, Gaddafi formulated his own particular approach to Arab nationalism and socialism, known as <a href="/wiki/Third_International_Theory" title="Third International Theory">Third International Theory</a>, which has been described as a combination of "<a href="/wiki/Utopian_socialism" title="Utopian socialism">utopian socialism</a>, Arab nationalism, and the Third World revolutionary theory that was in vogue at the time".<sup id="cite_ref-nytimes_green_294-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nytimes_green-294"><span>[</span>292<span>]</span></a></sup> He laid out the principles of this Theory in the three volumes of <i><a href="/wiki/The_Green_Book_(Libya)" title="The Green Book (Libya)" class="mw-redirect">The Green Book</a></i>, in which he sought to "explain the structure of the ideal society."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198657_295-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198657-295"><span>[</span>293<span>]</span></a></sup> His Arab nationalist views led him to believe that there needed to be unity across the Arab world, combining the Arab nation under a single nation-state.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198659_296-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198659-296"><span>[</span>294<span>]</span></a></sup> He described his approach to economics as "Islamic socialism",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198648_297-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198648-297"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup> although biographers Blundy and Lycett noted that Gaddafi's socialism had a "curiously <a href="/wiki/Marxism" title="Marxism">Marxist</a> undertone",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198798_298-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198798-298"><span>[</span>296<span>]</span></a></sup> with political scientist Sami Hajjar arguing that Gaddafi's model of socialism offered a simplification of <a href="/wiki/Karl_Marx" title="Karl Marx">Karl Marx</a> and <a href="/wiki/Friedrich_Engels" title="Friedrich Engels">Friedrich Engels</a>' theories.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHajjar1982_299-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHajjar1982-299"><span>[</span>297<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi saw his socialist <i>Jamahiriyah</i> as a model for the Arab, Islamic, and non-aligned worlds to follow.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198654_300-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198654-300"><span>[</span>298<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="quotebox" style="float: right; clear: right; margin: 0.5em 0 0.8em 1.4em; width:246px; padding: 6px; border: 1px solid #aaa; font-size: 88%; background-color: #ACE1AF;">
<div class="" style="position: relative; text-align: left;">
<div>
<p>"We call it the Third [International] Theory to indicate that there is a new path for all those who reject both materialist capitalism and atheist Communism. The path is for all the people of the world who abhor the dangerous confrontation between the Warsaw and North Atlantic military alliances. It is for all those who believe that all nations of the world are brothers under the aegis of the rule of God."</p>
</div>
</div>
<div style="text-align: right;"><cite>— Muammar Gaddafi.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198787_301-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198787-301"><span>[</span>299<span>]</span></a></sup></cite></div>
</div>
<p>Gaddafi's ideological worldview was moulded by his environment, namely his Islamic faith, his Bedouin upbringing, and his disgust at the actions of European colonialists in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198643_302-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198643-302"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> He was driven by a sense of "divine mission", believing himself a conduit of <a href="/wiki/God_in_Islam" title="God in Islam">Allah</a>'s will, and thought that he must achieve his goals "no matter what the cost".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198645.2C_50_303-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198645.2C_50-303"><span>[</span>301<span>]</span></a></sup> Raised within the <a href="/wiki/Sunni_Islam" title="Sunni Islam">Sunni</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>, Gaddafi called for the implementation of <i><a href="/wiki/Sharia" title="Sharia">sharia</a></i> within Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-Mohamed_Eljhami_304-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mohamed_Eljhami-304"><span>[</span>302<span>]</span></a></sup> He desired unity across the Islamic world,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719_305-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719-305"><span>[</span>303<span>]</span></a></sup> and encouraged the propagation of the faith elsewhere. On a 2010 visit to Italy, he paid a modelling agency to find 200 young Italian women for a lecture he gave urging them to convert.<sup id="cite_ref-306" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-306"><span>[</span>304<span>]</span></a></sup> He also funded the construction and renovation of two mosques in Africa, including Uganda's <a href="/wiki/Kampala_Mosque" title="Kampala Mosque" class="mw-redirect">Kampala Mosque</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-Thome_307-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thome-307"><span>[</span>305<span>]</span></a></sup> He nevertheless clashed with conservative Libyan clerics as to his interpretation of Islam. Many criticised his attempts to encourage women to enter traditionally male-only sectors of society, such as the armed forces. Gaddafi was keen to improve women's status, though saw the sexes as "separate but equal" and therefore felt women should usually remain in traditional roles.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198633.2C_53_308-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198633.2C_53-308"><span>[</span>306<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A fundamental part of Gaddafi's ideology was <a href="/wiki/Anti-Zionism" title="Anti-Zionism">anti-Zionism</a>. He believed that the state of Israel should not exist, and that any Arab compromise with the Israeli government was a betrayal of the Arab people.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198654BlundyLycett198718_309-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198654BlundyLycett198718-309"><span>[</span>307<span>]</span></a></sup> In large part due to their support of Israel, Gaddafi despised the United States, considering the country to be <a href="/wiki/Imperialism" title="Imperialism">imperialist</a> and lambasting it as "the embodiment of evil."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719.2C_197_310-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719.2C_197-310"><span>[</span>308<span>]</span></a></sup> Rallying against Jews in many of his speeches, his <a href="/wiki/Anti-Semitism" title="Anti-Semitism" class="mw-redirect">anti-Semitism</a> has been described as "almost <a href="/wiki/Adolf_Hitler" title="Adolf Hitler">Hitlerian</a>."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198725_311-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198725-311"><span>[</span>309<span>]</span></a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (May 2015)">by whom?</span></a></i>]</sup> From the late 1990s onward, his view seemed to become more moderate.<sup id="cite_ref-nymakeover_312-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nymakeover-312"><span>[</span>310<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2007, he advocated the <a href="/wiki/Isratin" title="Isratin">Isratin</a> <a href="/wiki/One-state_solution" title="One-state solution">single-state solution</a> to the <a href="/wiki/Israeli%E2%80%93Palestinian_conflict" title="Israeli–Palestinian conflict">Israeli–Palestinian conflict</a>, stating that "the [Israel-Palestine] solution is to establish a democratic state for the Jews and the Palestinians...This is the fundamental solution, or else the Jews will be annihilated in the future, because the Palestinians have [strategic] depth."<sup id="cite_ref-313" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-313"><span>[</span>311<span>]</span></a></sup> Two years later he argued that a single-state solution would "move beyond old conflicts and look to a unified future based on shared culture and respect."<sup id="cite_ref-314" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-314"><span>[</span>312<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_and_family_life">Personal and family life</span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_Gaddafi,_Damascus_University,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/170px-Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg" width="170" height="222" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/255px-Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg/340px-Muammar_Gaddafi%2C_Damascus_University%2C_Damascus%2C_Syria.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2297" data-file-height="3000" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Muammar_Gaddafi,_Damascus_University,_Damascus,_Syria.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi with an <a href="/wiki/Amazonian_Guard" title="Amazonian Guard">Amazonian Guard</a> in Damascus, April 2009</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gaddafi was a very private individual,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198643_302-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198643-302"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> who described himself as a "simple revolutionary" and "pious Muslim" called upon by <a href="/wiki/Allah" title="Allah">Allah</a> to continue Nasser's work.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198648_297-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198648-297"><span>[</span>295<span>]</span></a></sup> Reporter Mirella Bianco found that his friends considered him particularly loyal and generous, and asserted that he adored children.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBianco197510.E2.80.9312_315-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBianco197510.E2.80.9312-315"><span>[</span>313<span>]</span></a></sup> She was told by Gaddafi's father that even as a child he had been "always serious, even taciturn", a trait he also exhibited in adulthood.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBianco19757_316-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBianco19757-316"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup> His father said that he was courageous, intelligent, pious, and family oriented.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBianco19757_316-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBianco19757-316"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Other sources describe Gaddafi as "extraordinarily vain"<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724_317-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724-317"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup> and a womaniser.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198653.E2.80.9354BlundyLycett198722.E2.80.9323_318-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198653.E2.80.9354BlundyLycett198722.E2.80.9323-318"><span>[</span>316<span>]</span></a></sup> Blundy and Lycett note Gaddafi had a large wardrobe, and sometimes changed his outfit multiple times a day.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724_317-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724-317"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup> He saw himself as a <a href="/wiki/Fashion" title="Fashion">fashion</a> icon, stating "Whatever I wear becomes a fad. I wear a certain shirt and suddenly everyone is wearing it."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724_317-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198724-317"><span>[</span>315<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>In the 1970s and 1980s there were reports of his making sexual advances toward female reporters and members of his entourage.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198653.E2.80.9354BlundyLycett198722.E2.80.9323_318-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198653.E2.80.9354BlundyLycett198722.E2.80.9323-318"><span>[</span>316<span>]</span></a></sup> After the civil war, more serious charges came to light. <a href="/w/index.php?title=Annick_Cojean&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" class="new" title="Annick Cojean (page does not exist)">Annick Cojean</a><span class="noprint" style="font-size: smaller; font-style: normal;">&#160;(<a href="//fr.wikipedia.org/wiki/Annick_Cojean" class="extiw" title="fr:Annick Cojean">fr</a>)</span>, a journalist for <i><a href="/wiki/Le_Monde" title="Le Monde">Le Monde</a></i>, wrote in her book, <i>Gaddafi's Harem</i> that Gaddafi had raped, tortured, performed <a href="/wiki/Urolagnia" title="Urolagnia">urolagnia</a>, and imprisoned hundreds or thousands of women, usually very young.<sup id="cite_ref-harem_319-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harem-319"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup> Another source—Libyan psychologist Seham Sergewa—reported that several of his female bodyguards claim to have been <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">raped</a> by Gaddafi and senior officials.<sup id="cite_ref-tele1_320-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tele1-320"><span>[</span>318<span>]</span></a></sup> After the civil war, <a href="/wiki/Luis_Moreno-Ocampo" title="Luis Moreno-Ocampo" class="mw-redirect">Luis Moreno-Ocampo</a>, prosecutor for the International Criminal Court, said there was evidence that Gaddafi told soldiers to rape women who had spoken out against his regime.<sup id="cite_ref-tele1_320-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-tele1-320"><span>[</span>318<span>]</span></a></sup> In 2011 <a href="/wiki/Amnesty_International" title="Amnesty International">Amnesty</a> questioned this and other claims used to justify Nato's war in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-Amnesty.3F_321-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Amnesty.3F-321"><span>[</span>319<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>A group of Ukrainian female nurses who served Gaddafi had a different perspective of him. According to them, the only time they ever touched him was to take his blood pressure. In fact, they had nicknamed him "Papik", which meant "little father" in Russian.<sup id="cite_ref-322" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-322"><span>[</span>320<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>According to a Brazilian <a href="/wiki/Plastic_surgery" title="Plastic surgery">plastic surgeon</a>, Gaddafi had been his patient in 1995.<sup id="cite_ref-323" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-323"><span>[</span>321<span>]</span></a></sup> The U.S. <a href="/wiki/Central_Intelligence_Agency" title="Central Intelligence Agency">Central Intelligence Agency</a> believed that Gaddafi had suffered from <a href="/wiki/Clinical_depression" title="Clinical depression" class="mw-redirect">clinical depression</a>, while the Israeli authorities claimed that he had been afflicted by <a href="/wiki/Epilepsy" title="Epilepsy">epilepsy</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hemorrhoid" title="Hemorrhoid">hemorrhoids</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198721_324-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198721-324"><span>[</span>322<span>]</span></a></sup> He was a fan of <a href="/wiki/Beethoven" title="Beethoven" class="mw-redirect">Beethoven</a>, and said his favourite novels were <i><a href="/wiki/Uncle_Tom%27s_Cabin" title="Uncle Tom's Cabin">Uncle Tom's Cabin</a></i>, <i><a href="/wiki/Roots:_The_Saga_of_an_American_Family" title="Roots: The Saga of an American Family">Roots</a></i>, and <a href="/wiki/Colin_Wilson" title="Colin Wilson">Colin Wilson</a>'s <i><a href="/wiki/The_Outsider_(Colin_Wilson)" title="The Outsider (Colin Wilson)">The Outsider</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198722_325-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198722-325"><span>[</span>323<span>]</span></a></sup> He was also a <a href="/wiki/Association_football" title="Association football">football</a> enthusiast.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198722_325-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198722-325"><span>[</span>323<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following his ascension to power, Gaddafi moved into the <a href="/wiki/Bab_al-Azizia" title="Bab al-Azizia">Bab al-Azizia</a> barracks, a six-mile long fortified compound located two miles from the center of Tripoli. His home and office at Azizia was a bunker designed by West German engineers, while the rest of his family lived in a large two-story building. Within the compound were also two tennis courts, a soccer field, several gardens, camels, and a Bedouin tent in which he entertained guests.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19871_326-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett19871-326"><span>[</span>324<span>]</span></a></sup> In the 1980s, his lifestyle was considered modest in comparison to those of many other Arab leaders.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198732_327-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198732-327"><span>[</span>325<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi allegedly worked for years with Swiss banks to launder international banking transactions.<sup id="cite_ref-risen_328-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-risen-328"><span>[</span>326<span>]</span></a></sup> In November 2011, <i>The Sunday Times</i> identified property worth £1 billion in the UK that Gaddafi allegedly owned.<sup id="cite_ref-329" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-329"><span>[</span>327<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi had an <a href="/wiki/Airbus_A340" title="Airbus A340">Airbus A340</a> <a href="/wiki/Private_jet" title="Private jet" class="mw-redirect">private jet</a>, which he bought from <a href="/wiki/Al-Waleed_bin_Talal" title="Al-Waleed bin Talal">Prince Al-Waleed bin Talal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Saudi_Arabia" title="Saudi Arabia">Saudi Arabia</a> for $120 million in 2003.<sup id="cite_ref-330" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-330"><span>[</span>328<span>]</span></a></sup> Operated by <a href="/wiki/Tripoli" title="Tripoli">Tripoli</a>-based <a href="/wiki/Afriqiyah_Airways" title="Afriqiyah Airways">Afriqiyah Airways</a> and decorated externally in their colours, it had various luxuries including a <a href="/wiki/Jacuzzi" title="Jacuzzi">jacuzzi</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-331" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-331"><span>[</span>329<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi married his first wife, Fatiha al-Nuri, in 1969. She was the daughter of General Khalid, a senior figure in King Idris' administration, and was from a middle-class background. Although they had one son, <a href="/wiki/Muhammad_Gaddafi" title="Muhammad Gaddafi">Muhammad Gaddafi</a> (b. 1970), their relationship was strained, and they divorced in 1970.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198653BlundyLycett198722_332-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198653BlundyLycett198722-332"><span>[</span>330<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi's second wife was <a href="/wiki/Safia_Farkash" title="Safia Farkash">Safia Farkash</a>, <span title="name before marriage" style="border-bottom:1px dotted">née</span> el-Brasai, a former nurse from Obeidat tribe born in <a href="/wiki/Bayda,_Libya" title="Bayda, Libya">Bayda</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-cbcnews_333-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-cbcnews-333"><span>[</span>331<span>]</span></a></sup> They met in 1969, following his ascension to power, when he was hospitalized with appendicitis; he claimed that it was love at first sight.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198653BlundyLycett198722_332-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198653BlundyLycett198722-332"><span>[</span>330<span>]</span></a></sup> The couple remained married until his death. Together they had seven biological children: <a href="/wiki/Saif_al-Islam_Gaddafi" title="Saif al-Islam Gaddafi">Saif al-Islam Gaddafi</a> (b. 1972), <a href="/wiki/Al-Saadi_Gaddafi" title="Al-Saadi Gaddafi">Al-Saadi Gaddafi</a> (b. 1973), <a href="/wiki/Mutassim_Gaddafi" title="Mutassim Gaddafi">Mutassim Gaddafi</a> (1974–2011), <a href="/wiki/Hannibal_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="Hannibal Muammar Gaddafi">Hannibal Muammar Gaddafi</a> (b. 1975), <a href="/wiki/Ayesha_Gaddafi" title="Ayesha Gaddafi">Ayesha Gaddafi</a> (b. 1976), <a href="/wiki/Saif_al-Arab_Gaddafi" title="Saif al-Arab Gaddafi">Saif al-Arab Gaddafi</a> (1982–2011), and <a href="/wiki/Khamis_Gaddafi" title="Khamis Gaddafi">Khamis Gaddafi</a> (1983–2011?). He also adopted two children, <a href="/wiki/Hanna_Gaddafi" title="Hanna Gaddafi">Hanna Gaddafi</a> and Milad Gaddafi.<sup id="cite_ref-334" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-334"><span>[</span>332<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Public_image">Public image</span></h3>
<p>A <a href="/wiki/Cult_of_personality" title="Cult of personality">cult of personality</a> devoted to Gaddafi existed in Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720_335-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720-335"><span>[</span>333<span>]</span></a></sup> His face appeared on a wide variety of items, including postage stamps, watches, and school satchels. Quotations from <i>The Green Book</i> appeared on a wide variety of places, from street walls to airports and pens, and were put to <a href="/wiki/Pop_music" title="Pop music">pop music</a> for public release. Gaddafi claimed that he disliked this personality cult, but that he tolerated it because Libya's people adored him.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720_335-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720-335"><span>[</span>333<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg/250px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg" width="250" height="170" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg/375px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg/500px-Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg 2x" data-file-width="530" data-file-height="360" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Vladimir_Putin_and_Muammar_Gaddafi_in_Moscow_2_Nov_2008-2.jpeg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
During his 2008 visit to <a href="/wiki/Russia" title="Russia">Russia</a>, Gaddafi pitched his Bedouin tent in the grounds of the <a href="/wiki/Moscow_Kremlin" title="Moscow Kremlin">Moscow Kremlin</a>. Here he is joined by <a href="/wiki/Russian_Prime_Minister" title="Russian Prime Minister" class="mw-redirect">Russian Prime Minister</a> <a href="/wiki/Vladimir_Putin" title="Vladimir Putin">Vladimir Putin</a> and French singer <a href="/wiki/Mireille_Mathieu" title="Mireille Mathieu">Mireille Mathieu</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Biographers Blundy and Lycett believed that he was "a <a href="/wiki/Populism" title="Populism">populist</a> at heart."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720_335-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198720-335"><span>[</span>333<span>]</span></a></sup> Throughout Libya, crowds of supporters would turn up to public events at which he appeared; described as "spontaneous demonstrations" by the government, there are recorded instances of groups being coerced or paid to attend.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198716_336-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198716-336"><span>[</span>334<span>]</span></a></sup> He was typically late to public events, and would sometimes not show up at all.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198717_337-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198717-337"><span>[</span>335<span>]</span></a></sup> Although Bianco thought he had a "gift for oratory",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBianco19757_316-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBianco19757-316"><span>[</span>314<span>]</span></a></sup> he was considered a poor orator by biographers Blundy and Lycett.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198718_80-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198718-80"><span>[</span>78<span>]</span></a></sup> Biographer <a href="/wiki/Daniel_Kawczynski" title="Daniel Kawczynski">Daniel Kawczynski</a> noted that Gaddafi was famed for his "lengthy, wandering" speeches,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011191_338-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011191-338"><span>[</span>336<span>]</span></a></sup> which typically involved criticising Israel and the U.S.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198717_337-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198717-337"><span>[</span>335<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi was notably confrontational in his approach to foreign powers,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198651_339-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198651-339"><span>[</span>337<span>]</span></a></sup> and generally shunned western ambassadors and diplomats, believing them to be spies.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198721_324-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198721-324"><span>[</span>322<span>]</span></a></sup> He once said that HIV was "a peaceful virus, not an aggressive virus" and assured attendees at the <a href="/wiki/African_Union" title="African Union">African Union</a> that "if you are <a href="/wiki/Heterosexuality" title="Heterosexuality">straight</a> you have nothing to fear from AIDS".<sup id="cite_ref-rulebreaking_340-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rulebreaking-340"><span>[</span>338<span>]</span></a></sup> He also said that the <a href="/wiki/Influenza_A_virus_subtype_H1N1" title="Influenza A virus subtype H1N1">H1N1</a> <a href="/wiki/Influenza" title="Influenza">influenza</a> virus was a biological weapon manufactured by a foreign military, and he assured Africans that the <a href="/wiki/Tsetse_fly" title="Tsetse fly">tsetse fly</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mosquito" title="Mosquito">mosquito</a> were "God's armies which will protect us against colonialists". Should these 'enemies' come to Africa, "they will get <a href="/wiki/Malaria" title="Malaria">malaria</a> and <a href="/wiki/African_trypanosomiasis" title="African trypanosomiasis">sleeping sickness</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-rulebreaking_340-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rulebreaking-340"><span>[</span>338<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Gaddafi was preoccupied with his own security, regularly changing where he slept and sometimes grounding all other planes in Libya when he was flying.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198650_112-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198650-112"><span>[</span>110<span>]</span></a></sup> He made very particular requests when traveling to foreign nations. During his trips to Rome, Paris, Moscow, and New York,<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews_341-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews-341"><span>[</span>339<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-342" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-342"><span>[</span>340<span>]</span></a></sup> he resided in a bulletproof tent, following his Bedouin traditions.<sup id="cite_ref-abcnews_341-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-abcnews-341"><span>[</span>339<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-343" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-343"><span>[</span>341<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Starting in the 1980s, he travelled with his all-female <a href="/wiki/Amazonian_Guard" title="Amazonian Guard">Amazonian Guard</a>, who were allegedly sworn to a life of celibacy.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987112_117-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987112-117"><span>[</span>115<span>]</span></a></sup> However, according to psychologist Seham Sergewa, after the civil war several of the guards told her they had been pressured into joining and <a href="/wiki/Rape" title="Rape">raped</a> by Gaddafi and senior officials.<sup id="cite_ref-rape_344-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rape-344"><span>[</span>342<span>]</span></a></sup> He hired several Ukrainian nurses to care for him and his family's health,<sup id="cite_ref-345" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-345"><span>[</span>343<span>]</span></a></sup> and traveled everywhere with his trusted Ukrainian nurse <a href="/wiki/Halyna_Kolotnytska" title="Halyna Kolotnytska">Halyna Kolotnytska</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-346" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-346"><span>[</span>344<span>]</span></a></sup> Kolotnytska's daughter denied the suggestion that the relationship was anything but professional.<sup id="cite_ref-347" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-347"><span>[</span>345<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reception_and_legacy">Reception and legacy</span></h2>
<div class="hatnote relarticle mainarticle">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/International_reactions_to_the_death_of_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="International reactions to the death of Muammar Gaddafi">International reactions to the death of Muammar Gaddafi</a> and <a href="/wiki/List_of_awards_and_honours_bestowed_upon_Muammar_Gaddafi" title="List of awards and honours bestowed upon Muammar Gaddafi">List of awards and honours bestowed upon Muammar Gaddafi</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg/200px-Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg" width="200" height="277" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg/300px-Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d7/Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg/400px-Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1175" data-file-height="1625" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Africa_for_Africans_-_5th_extraordinary_session_of_the_Assembly_of_Heads_of_state_and_government.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Gaddafi on stamp: "Africa for Africans"</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Gaddafi remained a controversial and divisive figure on the world stage throughout his life and after death. Supporters praised Gaddafi's administration for the creation of an almost classless society through domestic reform.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200_348-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200-348"><span>[</span>346<span>]</span></a></sup> They stress the regime's achievements in combating homelessness and ensuring access to food and safe drinking water. Highlighting that under Gaddafi, all Libyans enjoyed free education to a university level, they point to the dramatic rise in literacy rates after the 1969 revolution.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200_348-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200-348"><span>[</span>346<span>]</span></a></sup> Supporters have also applauded achievements in medical care, praising the universal free healthcare provided under the Gaddafist administration, with diseases like <a href="/wiki/Cholera" title="Cholera">cholera</a> and <a href="/wiki/Typhoid" title="Typhoid" class="mw-redirect">typhoid</a> being contained and life expectancy raised.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200_348-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198719Kawczynski2011196.E2.80.93200-348"><span>[</span>346<span>]</span></a></sup> Biographers Blundy and Lycett believed that under the first decade of Gaddafi's leadership, life for most Libyans "undoubtedly changed for the better" as material conditions and wealth drastically improved,<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987107_71-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987107-71"><span>[</span>69<span>]</span></a></sup> while Libyan studies specialist Lillian Craig Harris remarked that in the early years of his administration, Libya's "national wealth and international influence soared, and its national standard of living has risen dramatically."<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198663_349-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198663-349"><span>[</span>347<span>]</span></a></sup> Such high standards declined during the 1980s, as a result of economic stagnation.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198668_350-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198668-350"><span>[</span>348<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi claimed that his <i>Jamahiriya</i> was a "concrete <a href="/wiki/Utopia" title="Utopia">utopia</a>", and that he had been appointed by "popular assent",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715_351-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715-351"><span>[</span>349<span>]</span></a></sup> with some Islamic supporters believing that he exhibited <i><a href="/wiki/Barakah" title="Barakah">barakah</a></i>.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198643_302-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198643-302"><span>[</span>300<span>]</span></a></sup> His opposition to Western governments earned him the respect of many in the Euro-American <a href="/wiki/Far_right" title="Far right" class="mw-redirect">far right</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-352" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-352"><span>[</span>350<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Critics labelled Gaddafi "despotic, cruel, arrogant, vain and stupid",<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198731_353-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198731-353"><span>[</span>351<span>]</span></a></sup> with western governments and press presenting him as the "vicious dictator of an oppressed people".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715_351-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198715-351"><span>[</span>349<span>]</span></a></sup> During the Reagan administration, the United States regarded him as "Public Enemy No. 1"<sup id="cite_ref-nymakeover_312-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nymakeover-312"><span>[</span>310<span>]</span></a></sup> and Reagan famously dubbed him the "mad dog of the Middle East".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011115.E2.80.93116.2C_120Bruce_St._John2012179.E2.80.93180_180-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011115.E2.80.93116.2C_120Bruce_St._John2012179.E2.80.93180-180"><span>[</span>178<span>]</span></a></sup> According to critics, the Libyan people lived in a climate of fear under Gaddafi's administration, due to his government's pervasive surveillance of civilians.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHarris198668BlundyLycett198729Kawczynski2011196.2C_208_354-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHarris198668BlundyLycett198729Kawczynski2011196.2C_208-354"><span>[</span>352<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi's Libya was typically described by western commentators as "a <a href="/wiki/Police_state" title="Police state">police state</a>".<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198728_355-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett198728-355"><span>[</span>353<span>]</span></a></sup> Opponents were critical of Libya's human rights abuses; according to Human Rights Watch (HRW) and others, hundreds of arrested political opponents often failed to receive a <a href="/wiki/Fair_trial" title="Fair trial" class="mw-redirect">fair trial</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-356" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-356"><span>[</span>354<span>]</span></a></sup> and were sometimes subjected to torture or extrajudicial execution, most notably in the <a href="/wiki/Abu_Salim_prison" title="Abu Salim prison">Abu Salim prison</a>, including an alleged massacre on 29 June 1996 in which HRW estimated that 1,270 prisoners were massacred.<sup id="cite_ref-Human_Rights_Watch_1270_357-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Human_Rights_Watch_1270-357"><span>[</span>355<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011210.E2.80.93212_358-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011210.E2.80.93212-358"><span>[</span>356<span>]</span></a></sup> Dissidents abroad or "stray dogs" were also publicly threatened with death and sometimes killed by government hit squads.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987133.E2.80.93138Vandewalle200827Bruce_St._John2012171_359-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBlundyLycett1987133.E2.80.93138Vandewalle200827Bruce_St._John2012171-359"><span>[</span>357<span>]</span></a></sup> His government's treatment of non-Arab Libyans has also came in for criticism from human rights activists, with native Berbers, Italians, Jews, refugees, and foreign workers all facing persecution in Gaddafist Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011202.E2.80.93203.2C_209_360-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKawczynski2011202.E2.80.93203.2C_209-360"><span>[</span>358<span>]</span></a></sup> According to journalist Annick Cojean and psychologist Seham Sergewa, Gaddafi and senior officials raped and imprisoned hundreds or thousands of young women and reportedly raped several of his female bodyguards.<sup id="cite_ref-harem_319-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-harem-319"><span>[</span>317<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-rape_344-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-rape-344"><span>[</span>342<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi's government was frequently criticized for not being democratic, with <a href="/wiki/Freedom_House" title="Freedom House">Freedom House</a> consistently giving Libya under Gaddafi the "Not Free" ranking for civil liberties and political rights.<sup id="cite_ref-FH1972_361-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FH1972-361"><span>[</span>359<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<div class="thumb tleft">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Libya.svg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Libya.svg/220px-Flag_of_Libya.svg.png" width="220" height="110" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Libya.svg/330px-Flag_of_Libya.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/05/Flag_of_Libya.svg/440px-Flag_of_Libya.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="480" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Flag_of_Libya.svg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
The pre-Gaddafi flag of Libya, readopted in the aftermath of the civil war</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>International reactions to Gaddafi's death were divided. US President <a href="/wiki/Barack_Obama" title="Barack Obama">Barack Obama</a> stated that it meant that "the shadow of tyranny over Libya has been lifted,"<sup id="cite_ref-362" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-362"><span>[</span>360<span>]</span></a></sup> while UK Prime Minister <a href="/wiki/David_Cameron" title="David Cameron">David Cameron</a> stated that he was "proud" of his country's role in overthrowing "this brutal dictator".<sup id="cite_ref-363" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-363"><span>[</span>361<span>]</span></a></sup> Contrastingly, former Cuban President <a href="/wiki/Fidel_Castro" title="Fidel Castro">Fidel Castro</a> commented that in defying the rebels, Gaddafi would "enter history as one of the great figures of the Arab nations",<sup id="cite_ref-364" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-364"><span>[</span>362<span>]</span></a></sup> while Venezuelan President <a href="/wiki/Hugo_Ch%C3%A1vez" title="Hugo Chávez">Hugo Chávez</a> described him as "a great fighter, a revolutionary and a martyr."<sup id="cite_ref-365" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-365"><span>[</span>363<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Nelson_Mandela" title="Nelson Mandela">Nelson Mandela</a> expressed sadness at the news, praising Gaddafi for his anti-apartheid stance, remarking that he backed the <a href="/wiki/African_National_Congress" title="African National Congress">African National Congress</a> during "the darkest moments of our struggle".<sup id="cite_ref-bbc_africa_366-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc_africa-366"><span>[</span>364<span>]</span></a></sup> Gaddafi was mourned by many as a hero across Sub-Saharan Africa,<sup id="cite_ref-367" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-367"><span>[</span>365<span>]</span></a></sup> for instance, a vigil was held by Muslims in Sierra Leone.<sup id="cite_ref-368" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-368"><span>[</span>366<span>]</span></a></sup> The <i><a href="/wiki/Daily_Times_of_Nigeria" title="Daily Times of Nigeria">Daily Times of Nigeria</a></i> stated that while undeniably a dictator, Gaddafi was the most benevolent in a region that only knew dictatorship, and that he was "a great man that looked out for his people and made them the envy of all of Africa."<sup id="cite_ref-dailytimes_ng_369-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-dailytimes_ng-369"><span>[</span>367<span>]</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/AllAfrica.com" title="AllAfrica.com">AllAfrica.com</a> reported that while many Libyans and Africans would mourn Gaddafi, this would be ignored by western media and that as such it would take 50 years before historians decided whether he was "martyr or villain."<sup id="cite_ref-370" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-370"><span>[</span>368<span>]</span></a></sup></p>
<p>Following his defeat in the civil war, Gaddafi's system of governance was dismantled and replaced under the interim government of the NTC, who legalised trade unions and freedom of the press. In July 2012, <a href="/wiki/Libyan_General_National_Congress_election,_2012" title="Libyan General National Congress election, 2012">elections were held</a> to form a new <a href="/wiki/General_National_Congress" title="General National Congress">General National Congress</a> (GNC), who officially took over governance from the NTC in August. The GNC proceeded to elect <a href="/wiki/Mohammed_Magariaf" title="Mohammed Magariaf">Mohammed Magariaf</a> as president of the chamber, and then voted <a href="/wiki/Mustafa_A.G._Abushagur" title="Mustafa A.G. Abushagur">Mustafa A.G. Abushagur</a> as <a href="/wiki/Prime_Minister_of_Libya" title="Prime Minister of Libya" class="mw-redirect">Prime Minister</a>; when Abushagar failed to gain congressional approval, the GNC instead elected <a href="/wiki/Ali_Zeidan" title="Ali Zeidan">Ali Zeidan</a> to the position.<sup id="cite_ref-371" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-371"><span>[</span>369<span>]</span></a></sup> In January 2013, the GNC officially renamed the <i>Jamahiriyah</i> as the "State of Libya".<sup id="cite_ref-Herald_StateofLibya_372-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Herald_StateofLibya-372"><span>[</span>370<span>]</span></a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="References">References</span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span></h3>
<div class="reflist" style="list-style-type: lower-alpha;">
<ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">For purposes of this article, 23 August 2011 is considered to be the date that Gaddafi left office. Other dates might have been chosen.</span>
<ul>
<li><span class="reference-text">On 15 July 2011, at a meeting in Istanbul, more than 30 governments, including the United States, withdrew recognition from Gaddafi's government and recognised the National Transitional Council (NTC) as the legitimate government of Libya.<sup id="cite_ref-INDtncofficialgov_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INDtncofficialgov-1"><span>[</span>1<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
<li>On 23 August 2011, during the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Tripoli_(2011)" title="Battle of Tripoli (2011)">Battle of Tripoli</a>, Gaddafi lost effective political and military control of Tripoli after his compound was captured by rebel forces.<sup id="cite_ref-liveblog23811_2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-liveblog23811-2"><span>[</span>2<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>On 25 August 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Arab_League" title="Arab League">Arab League</a> proclaimed the anti-Gaddafi National Transitional Council to be "the legitimate representative of the Libyan state".<sup id="cite_ref-taipeitimmes20110826_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-taipeitimmes20110826-3"><span>[</span>3<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>On 20 October 2011, Gaddafi was captured and killed near his hometown of Sirte.<sup id="cite_ref-bbc20111021_4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bbc20111021-4"><span>[</span>4<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
<li>In a ceremony on 23 October 2011, officials of the interim National Transitional Council declared, "We declare to the whole world that we have liberated our beloved country, with its cities, villages, hill-tops, mountains, deserts and skies."<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span>[</span>5<span>]</span></a></sup></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-14"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-14">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Due to the lack of standardization of <a href="/wiki/Romanization_of_Arabic" title="Romanization of Arabic">transcribing</a> <a href="/wiki/Written_Arabic" title="Written Arabic" class="mw-redirect">written</a> and <a href="/wiki/Varieties_of_Arabic#Regional_varieties" title="Varieties of Arabic">regionally</a> <a href="/wiki/Arabic_language#Colloquial_varieties" title="Arabic language" class="mw-redirect">pronounced</a> Arabic, Gaddafi's name has been <a href="/wiki/Romanization" title="Romanization">romanized</a> in various different ways. A 1986 column by <i><a href="/wiki/The_Straight_Dope" title="The Straight Dope">The Straight Dope</a></i> lists 32 spellings known from the U.S. <a href="/wiki/Library_of_Congress" title="Library of Congress">Library of Congress</a>,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8"><span>[</span>7<span>]</span></a></sup> while <a href="/wiki/American_Broadcasting_Company" title="American Broadcasting Company">ABC</a> and MSNBC identified 112 possible spellings.<sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9"><span>[</span>8<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span>[</span>9<span>]</span></a></sup> A 2007 interview with Gaddafi's son <a href="/wiki/Saif_al-Islam_Gaddafi" title="Saif al-Islam Gaddafi">Saif al-Islam Gaddafi</a> confirms that Saif spelled his own name "Qadhafi",<sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11"><span>[</span>10<span>]</span></a></sup> and the passport of Gaddafi's son Mohammed used the spelling "Al-Gathafi".<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12"><span>[</span>11<span>]</span></a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13"><span>[</span>12<span>]</span></a></sup></span></li>
</ol>
</div>
